Impossibility of Two Definitions and Order of Genus and Differentia
Commentary on the objection of two definitions and the order of genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why one thing cannot have two definitions and why the genus should precede the differentia in a complete definition.

Core Idea
A single essence can have only one genuine definition; the other is a description.

This passage from a commentary on definitions addresses a key objection: what if two definitions are proposed for the same thing? The commentator explains that if both are truly definitions, the first must be invalidated because one essence cannot have two definitions. He also discusses the proper order of genus and differentia, emphasizing that completeness requires both components, but the genus should come first for clarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated structure of 'his statement' and the explanation.
2
Understand the objection and its two scenarios
The passage presents a scenario: if a second definition is accepted as a true definition (ḥadd), then the first definition is invalidated. If not accepted, the first remains valid. Understand the logic: one essence cannot have two definitions.
The distinction between being a definition and being a description is key.
3
Learn the rule about ordering genus and differentia
The commentator notes that it is more proper (اولى) and appropriate (انسيب) to place the genus before the differentia. Delaying the genus does not harm completeness, but failing to combine both does.
This relates to the structure of a complete definition (الحد التام).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإن سلم الحد الثاني ) أي حديته وكذا قوله : بطل حده وقوله وإلا فلا. قوله : ( إذ لا اتحاد إلخ ) دليل لقوله بطل حده أي لا اتحاد بين المفهومين حتى يقال : إن كلا الحدين واحد من حيث المفهوم، فلا يلزم من حديتهما تعدد الماهية لشيء واحد، بل كل منهما مفهوم على حدة، فلا يمكن كونهما حدين، فإذا سلم حدية الثاني بطل حدية الأول، وفي بعض النسخ إذ لا تعاند بين إلخ، فيكون دليلاً لما يفهم من قوله بطل حده، أي لا يبطل كونه تعريفاً إذ لا تعاند بين مفهومي الحدين في الصدق، بل بينهما مغايرة في المفهوم، فيجوز أن يكون أحدهما حداً والآخر رسماً أو كلاهما رسماً. قوله : (أولى) فتأخير الجنس في الحد التام لا يخل بتماميته، إنما المخل به عدم تركيب أحدهما بالآخر. قوله : (أنسب) ليكون التخصيص بعد التعميم . قوله : ( فإن سلم الحد الثاني إلخ) أي إِن سلم حديته بطل حده، إذ لا يكون لشيء واحد حدان ، وإن لم يسلم لم يبطل حده بمجرد صدق المفهوم الثاني، إذ لا تعاند بين نفس مفهومي الحدين المذكورين إنما التعاند بين حديتهما، فيجوز أن يكون صدق أحدهما بطريق الحدية ويصدق الآخر صدقاً عرضياً. قوله : (إذ ليس للحد التام جزء خارج إلخ ) قال الأستاذ المحقق في شرح المطالع : اختلف أهل الفن في أن الهيئة الاجتماعية جزء للحد التام أم لا ، فالبعض على أنه جزء حتى لو قدم الفصل على الجنس لكان حداً ناقصاً، وقال الشريف : وهذا ليس بشيء والحق أنه لا جزء له غير الجنس والفصل، لكنه لا بد لمطابقته للذات من اجتماعهما، وما يكون تابعاً لذلك الاجتماع، لكنه لازم وهذا الكلام في غاية البعد إذ لا شبهة في أن جميع أجزاء الشيء نفسه، ولا يعقل انفكاك الشيء عن نفسه، فلو لم يكن للحد التام جزء غير الجنس والفصل للزم أن يتحقق الحد خارجي لازم عنه انتهى كلامه.
His statement: 'If the second definition is accepted' means its being a definition, and similarly his statement: 'his definition is invalidated' and his statement: 'and if not, then no.' His statement: 'Since there is no union, etc.' is evidence for his statement 'his definition is invalidated', meaning there is no union between the two concepts such that it could be said that both definitions are one in terms of concept, so it does not follow from their being definitions a multiplicity of essence for one thing; rather, each of them is a distinct concept, so they cannot be two definitions. If the second's being a definition is accepted, then the first's being a definition is invalidated. In some copies: 'since there is no opposition, etc.', so it is evidence for what is understood from his statement 'his definition is invalidated', i.e., it does not invalidate it being a definition, since there is no opposition between the two concepts of the two definitions in truth; rather, there is difference in concept, so it is permissible that one of them be a definition and the other a description, or both a description. His statement: 'more proper' - so delaying the genus in the complete definition does not harm its completeness; what harms it is the lack of composition of one with the other. His statement: 'more appropriate' - so that specification is after generalization. His statement: 'If the second definition is accepted, etc.' means if its being a definition is accepted, then his definition is invalidated, since one thing cannot have two definitions. And if it is not accepted, then his definition is not invalidated merely by the truth of the second concept, since there is no opposition between the concepts themselves of the two mentioned definitions; rather, the opposition is between their being definitions. So it is permissible that one of them be true in the manner of a definition and the other true accidentally. His statement: 'Since the complete definition has no part outside, etc.' The distinguished teacher said in Sharh al-Matali': The people of the art disagreed as to whether the social form is a part of the complete definition or not. Some hold that it is a part, so that if the differentia is presented before the genus, it would be a deficient definition. And al-Sharif said: This is nothing, and the truth is that it has no part other than the genus and differentia, but for it to correspond to the essence, their combination is necessary, and what is consequent upon that combination is necessary. And this speech is extremely distant, for there is no doubt that all parts of a thing are itself, and it is inconceivable that a thing be separated from itself. So if the complete definition had no part other than the genus and differentia, then the definition would be realized externally, a necessary consequence of it. End of his speech.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on objections regarding definitions (ḥudūd). The commentator first addresses the objection that if a second definition is accepted as a definition (ḥadd), then the first definition would be invalidated because two definitions cannot apply to one essence (dhāt). He explains that the two concepts (mafhūm) are distinct, so they can both be true, but only one can be a genuine definition; the other could be a mere description (rasm). He then discusses the order of genus (jins) and differentia (faṣl) in a complete definition: it is more appropriate to present the genus first because it is more general and known first, and the differentia is a specification (takhṣīṣ); delaying the genus does not harm completeness, but failure to combine both does. Finally, he addresses the claim that the complete definition has no part other than genus and differentia. He quotes a disagreement about whether the 'social form' (al-hay'a al-ijtimā'iyya) is a part. Al-Sharif (al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjānī) argues it is not a part, but the commentator criticizes this view as far-fetched, asserting that if the definition had no other part, it would be realized externally, which is necessarily entailed.

Key Terms

رسم
جنس
فصل
مفهوم
تعاند

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two different definitions can both be valid definitions for the same thing.
Correction: One thing can have at most one genuine definition; the other must be a description (rasm).

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what happens if a second definition (ḥadd) is accepted for the same thing?
A) Both are valid definitions.
B) The first definition is invalidated because one thing cannot have two definitions.
C) The second definition becomes a description.
D) The definitions are combined into one.

Key Takeaway
One essence cannot have two definitions; if a second definition is accepted, the first is invalidated. In a complete definition, the genus should precede the differentia for clarity, but the order does not affect completeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that one essence cannot have two genuine definitions. In your own life, can you identify a core belief or identity that you have tried to define in two contradictory ways? How does this logical principle challenge you to seek a single, coherent definition of who you are before Allah?
?
When you encounter someone who offers an alternative definition of a fundamental Islamic concept (e.g., 'tawḥīd'), do you quickly accept it as equally valid, or do you examine whether it is a true definition or merely a description? How will you apply the lesson's distinction between ḥadd and rasm to protect the purity of your faith?
?
The passage emphasizes that the order of genus and differentia, though more proper, does not affect completeness. Is the Social Form a Part of the Complete Definition?
Disagreement on whether social form is a part of complete definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among logicians about whether the 'social form' (al-hay'a al-ijtimā'iyya) is a part of the complete definition.

Core Idea
The social form resulting from combining genus and differentia is disputed as a necessary part of the definition.

This passage presents a disagreement among logicians regarding the complete definition. Some argue that the 'social form' (the structure resulting from the combination of genus and differentia) is a part of the definition, while others, like al-Sharif al-Jurjānī, claim it is not a part but a necessary concomitant. The commentator critiques al-Sharif's view as distant from reason, asserting that if the definition had no other part, it would be realized externally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the quoted disagreement.
Notice the structure: a statement from the text, then a quote from Sharh al-Matali', then commentary.
2
Understand the two positions
Identify the two views: some hold that the social form is a part of the complete definition; al-Sharif argues it is not a part but a necessary concomitant.
The social form is the structured combination of genus and differentia.
3
Grasp the commentator's critique
The commentator dismisses al-Sharif's view as extremely distant, arguing that if the definition had no part other than genus and differentia, it would be realized externally, which is impossible.
This critique suggests the social form must be considered a part.

قوله : ( فإن سلم الحد الثاني ) أي حديته وكذا قوله : بطل حده وقوله وإلا فلا. قوله : ( إذ لا اتحاد إلخ ) دليل لقوله بطل حده أي لا اتحاد بين المفهومين حتى يقال : إن كلا الحدين واحد من حيث المفهوم، فلا يلزم من حديتهما تعدد الماهية لشيء واحد، بل كل منهما مفهوم على حدة، فلا يمكن كونهما حدين، فإذا سلم حدية الثاني بطل حدية الأول، وفي بعض النسخ إذ لا تعاند بين إلخ، فيكون دليلاً لما يفهم من قوله بطل حده، أي لا يبطل كونه تعريفاً إذ لا تعاند بين مفهومي الحدين في الصدق، بل بينهما مغايرة في المفهوم، فيجوز أن يكون أحدهما حداً والآخر رسماً أو كلاهما رسماً. قوله : (أولى) فتأخير الجنس في الحد التام لا يخل بتماميته، إنما المخل به عدم تركيب أحدهما بالآخر. قوله : (أنسب) ليكون التخصيص بعد التعميم . قوله : ( فإن سلم الحد الثاني إلخ) أي إِن سلم حديته بطل حده، إذ لا يكون لشيء واحد حدان ، وإن لم يسلم لم يبطل حده بمجرد صدق المفهوم الثاني، إذ لا تعاند بين نفس مفهومي الحدين المذكورين إنما التعاند بين حديتهما، فيجوز أن يكون صدق أحدهما بطريق الحدية ويصدق الآخر صدقاً عرضياً. قوله : (إذ ليس للحد التام جزء خارج إلخ ) قال الأستاذ المحقق في شرح المطالع : اختلف أهل الفن في أن الهيئة الاجتماعية جزء للحد التام أم لا ، فالبعض على أنه جزء حتى لو قدم الفصل على الجنس لكان حداً ناقصاً، وقال الشريف : وهذا ليس بشيء والحق أنه لا جزء له غير الجنس والفصل، لكنه لا بد لمطابقته للذات من اجتماعهما، وما يكون تابعاً لذلك الاجتماع، لكنه لازم وهذا الكلام في غاية البعد إذ لا شبهة في أن جميع أجزاء الشيء نفسه، ولا يعقل انفكاك الشيء عن نفسه، فلو لم يكن للحد التام جزء غير الجنس والفصل للزم أن يتحقق الحد خارجي لازم عنه انتهى كلامه.
His statement: 'If the second definition is accepted' means its being a definition, and similarly his statement: 'his definition is invalidated' and his statement: 'and if not, then no.' His statement: 'Since there is no union, etc.' is evidence for his statement 'his definition is invalidated', meaning there is no union between the two concepts such that it could be said that both definitions are one in terms of concept, so it does not follow from their being definitions a multiplicity of essence for one thing; rather, each of them is a distinct concept, so they cannot be two definitions. If the second's being a definition is accepted, then the first's being a definition is invalidated. In some copies: 'since there is no opposition, etc.', so it is evidence for what is understood from his statement 'his definition is invalidated', i.e., it does not invalidate it being a definition, since there is no opposition between the two concepts of the two definitions in truth; rather, there is difference in concept, so it is permissible that one of them be a definition and the other a description, or both a description. His statement: 'more proper' - so delaying the genus in the complete definition does not harm its completeness; what harms it is the lack of composition of one with the other. His statement: 'more appropriate' - so that specification is after generalization. His statement: 'If the second definition is accepted, etc.' means if its being a definition is accepted, then his definition is invalidated, since one thing cannot have two definitions. And if it is not accepted, then his definition is not invalidated merely by the truth of the second concept, since there is no opposition between the concepts themselves of the two mentioned definitions; rather, the opposition is between their being definitions. So it is permissible that one of them be true in the manner of a definition and the other true accidentally. His statement: 'Since the complete definition has no part outside, etc.' The distinguished teacher said in Sharh al-Matali': The people of the art disagreed as to whether the social form is a part of the complete definition or not. Some hold that it is a part, so that if the differentia is presented before the genus, it would be a deficient definition. And al-Sharif said: This is nothing, and the truth is that it has no part other than the genus and differentia, but for it to correspond to the essence, their combination is necessary, and what is consequent upon that combination is necessary. And this speech is extremely distant, for there is no doubt that all parts of a thing are itself, and it is inconceivable that a thing be separated from itself. So if the complete definition had no part other than the genus and differentia, then the definition would be realized externally, a necessary consequence of it. End of his speech.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on objections regarding definitions (ḥudūd). The commentator first addresses the objection that if a second definition is accepted as a definition (ḥadd), then the first definition would be invalidated because two definitions cannot apply to one essence (dhāt). He explains that the two concepts (mafhūm) are distinct, so they can both be true, but only one can be a genuine definition; the other could be a mere description (rasm). He then discusses the order of genus (jins) and differentia (faṣl) in a complete definition: it is more appropriate to present the genus first because it is more general and known first, and the differentia is a specification (takhṣīṣ); delaying the genus does not harm completeness, but failure to combine both does. Finally, he addresses the claim that the complete definition has no part other than genus and differentia. He quotes a disagreement about whether the 'social form' (al-hay'a al-ijtimā'iyya) is a part. Al-Sharif (al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjānī) argues it is not a part, but the commentator criticizes this view as far-fetched, asserting that if the definition had no other part, it would be realized externally, which is necessarily entailed.

حد
جنس
فصل
جزء
لازم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The complete definition consists only of genus and differentia with no other part.
Correction: The passage argues against this view, implying that the social form is indeed a part of the definition (or at least not merely a concomitant).

Checkpoint
According to al-Sharif (al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjānī), what is the status of the 'social form' (al-hay'a al-ijtimā'iyya) in the complete definition?
A) It is a part of the definition.
B) It is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the combination of genus and differentia.
C) It is the differentia itself.
D) It is the genus itself.

Key Takeaway
The social form (the structured combination of genus and differentia) is considered a part of the definition by some logicians, but al-Sharif argues it is only a necessary concomitant. The commentator rejects this latter view as far-fetched.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage shows that even subtle distinctions about definitions have real implications. In your own learning, are you content with surface-level understanding, or do you push yourself to see the 'parts' that compose a concept? Can you name one area where you have settled for a vague definition?
?
The commentator calls a differing view 'extremely distant' – a strong dismissal. Reflect on how you handle intellectual disagreements. Do you dismiss opposing views quickly, or do you engage with them charitably to test your own position? Which approach leads to truth?

Avoiding Problematic Words in Definitions
Commentary on avoiding problematic words in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why definitions must avoid strange, ambiguous, and metaphorical words without clear context, and how scholars rank these in undesirability.

Core Idea
Clear definitions require avoiding words that confuse the listener.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif discusses the requirement that definitions use words that clearly indicate their intended meaning. The commentator explains three types of problematic words and their relative severity, resolving an apparent contradiction between two scholarly opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the dense commentary style.
The passage contains both the original matn and sharh.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three types of problematic words: strange (gharība waḥshiyya), ambiguous (mushtarak), and metaphorical (majāz). Understand the problem each causes.
The passage explains the listener's confusion for each.
3
Understand the ranking debate
Notice that two different rankings are given: one says metaphorical is worse than ambiguous, another says ambiguous is worse than metaphorical. Find which source says what.
One ranking is from Sharh al-Mukhtasar, the other from al-Matali'.
4
Resolve the contradiction
The commentator resolves the contradiction by considering the presence and effect of context (qarīna). Understand the two scenarios.
Context diverts from literal meaning or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ويحترز) فيه ( عن الألفاظ الغريبة الوحشية التي لا يفهم السامع معناها فيحتاج إلى تفسيرها فتطول المسافة، وذلك مما يختلف بالقياس إلى السامعين، فإن اصطلاحات كل قوم مشهورة عند أربابها غريبة عند غيرهم (وعن المشترك والمجاز بلا (قرينة ظاهرة فيتردد السامع حينئذ في المشترك بين المقصود ،وغيره ويتبادر ذهنه في المجاز إلى غيره ) وبالجملة فعن كل لفظ غير ظاهر الدلالة على المقصود ) وذلك لأنه بصدد الإظهار والتوضيح، فلا بد من ظهور الدلالة. قوله : ( فتطول المسافة ) فيه إشارة إلى أنه لا خلل في إفادة المراد . قوله : ( بلا قرينة (ظاهرة بأن لا تكون قرينة أو لا تكون ظاهرة . قوله : ( ويتبادر ذهنه في المجاز إلى غيره فيه إشارة إلى أن المجاز أردأ من المشترك، وبه صرح في حواشي المطالع من أن المشترك أردأ من المجاز ، فلعله بالنظر إلى الاستعمال، فإن استعمال المشترك والمجاز بلا قرينة غير جائز ، ومع ذلك استعمال المشترك أقل من المجاز . قوله : ( ويحترز عن الألفاظ الغريبة الوحشية وعن المشترك والمجاز بلا قرينة ذكر الشارح في حواشي شرح المختصر إِن هذه الثلاثة مرتبة في الرداءة فإن الألفاظ المشتركة أردأ من الغريبة، إذ لا يفهم من الألفاظ الغريبة شيء، فيحتاج إلى تفسيرها فتطول المسافة، وأيضاً الغرابة تختلف بحسب قوم وقوم، وفي الألفاظ المشتركة بلا قرينة معينة لأحد معانيها يتردد السامع بين المقصود، وغيره فلا يفهم المقصود بل ربما يفهم غيره والألفاظ المجازية أردأ من المشتركة إذ المجازية بلا قرينة صارفة عن المعنى الحقيقي ظاهرة في غير المقصود، فلا يفهم المقصود بل يتبادر الفهم إلى غير المقصود، ويقع الجهل وذكر في حواشي المطالع أن الألفاظ المشتركة أردأ من المجازية والمجازية أردأ من الغريبة الوحشية وبين كلاميه مخالفة ظاهرة لا يقال : عند عدم صرف القرينة عن الحقيقة، فالمجاز أرداً من المشترك كما ذكر في حواشي شرح المختصر، وعند الصرف وعدم تعيين المراد فالمشترك أرداً منه إذ فيه مزاحمة غير المقصود للمقصود،
'And one avoids in it strange and barbarous words whose meaning the listener does not understand, so he needs to interpret them, thus the distance becomes long; and that varies relative to listeners, for the technical terms of each group are well-known among its people but strange to others; and one avoids ambiguous and metaphorical words without a clear context, for then the listener wavers in the ambiguous between the intended and other, and his mind leaps in the metaphorical to other; and in summary, one avoids every word whose indication of the intended is not apparent, because it is in the position of making clear and clarifying, so indication must be apparent.' His statement: 'thus the distance becomes long' indicates that there is no defect in conveying the intended. His statement: 'without a clear context' means either there is no context or it is not apparent. His statement: 'and his mind leaps in the metaphorical to other' indicates that the metaphorical is worse than the ambiguous, and it is explicitly stated in the margins of al-Matali' that the ambiguous is worse than the metaphorical; perhaps this is with respect to usage, for using the ambiguous and metaphorical without context is not permissible, yet usage of the ambiguous is less than the metaphorical. His statement: 'and one avoids strange and barbarous words and ambiguous and metaphorical without a context' – the commentator mentioned in the margins of Sharh al-Mukhtasar that these three are ranked in badness: the ambiguous words are worse than the strange, because from the strange words nothing is understood, so they need interpretation, thus the distance becomes long; and also strangeness varies according to groups; and in the ambiguous words without a context specifying one of their meanings, the listener wavers between the intended and other, so he does not understand the intended, and perhaps he understands other; and the metaphorical words are worse than the ambiguous, because the metaphorical without a context diverting from the literal meaning is apparent in other than the intended, so he does not understand the intended, rather understanding leaps to other than the intended, and ignorance occurs. And it is mentioned in the margins of al-Matali' that the ambiguous words are worse than the metaphorical, and the metaphorical is worse than the strange barbarous; and there is apparent contradiction between his two statements. It is not said: when the context does not divert from the literal, the metaphorical is worse than the ambiguous as mentioned in the margins of Sharh al-Mukhtasar; and when it diverts and the intended is not specified, the ambiguous is worse than it, because in it the non-intended competes with the intended.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the requirement to avoid problematic words in definitions. The original text states that one must avoid strange (gharība waḥshiyya), ambiguous (mushtarak), and metaphorical (majāz) words without a clear context (qarīna). The commentator explains each: strange words require interpretation, lengthening the process; ambiguous words cause hesitation between meanings; metaphorical words lead the mind away from the intended. He then discusses the ranking of these words in undesirability. He notes a discrepancy: in the margins of al-Matali', ambiguous is said to be worse than metaphorical, but in the margins of Sharh al-Mukhtasar, metaphorical is worse than ambiguous. He resolves this by considering two scenarios: when there is no context to divert from the literal, metaphorical is worse; when context diverts but the intended is unspecified, ambiguous is worse because the non-intended competes with the intended.

غريبة وحشية
مشترك
مجاز
قرينة
رداءة
استعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All problematic words are equally bad in definitions.
Correction: The passage shows a nuanced ranking: ambiguous and metaphorical words are worse than strange, and their relative severity depends on the presence and effect of context.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, when is the metaphorical (majāz) worse than the ambiguous (mushtarak)?
A) When there is no context to divert from the literal meaning.
B) When the context diverts but the intended is not specified.
C) Always, because metaphorical words are never clear.
D) Never, because ambiguous is always worse.

Key Takeaway
The choice between ambiguous and metaphorical words in definitions depends on whether context diverts from the literal meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you explain Islamic concepts to others, do you check that your words are clear and not ambiguous? Have you ever caused confusion because you used a word with multiple meanings without context? What will you change after learning this?
?

Now, consider your own speech: Are you careful to avoid such confusion when speaking about Allah's attributes? Write down one step you will take to ensure your words are clear.
?
The passage shows that even scholars differed on ranking badness. When you encounter differences of opinion, do you seek to understand the underlying reasons, or do you dismiss them? How can this lesson teach you to be more precise in your own thinking and speech?

Definition of Qiyas and Its Essential Conditions
The author defines qiyas as a composite statement and explains its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of qiyas (syllogism) as a composite statement, its conditions, and the distinction between audible and intellectual qiyas.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a composite statement that entails another statement by itself.

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational definition of qiyas (syllogism), which is the core tool of reasoning in logic and theology. He clarifies that it is not just any statement but a composite one made of multiple propositions, and he specifies the condition that the conclusion must follow necessarily from the premises alone. This lesson will walk you through the definition and its subtle conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that qiyas is defined as a 'composite statement' (qawl mu’allaf). Identify the two types: audible and intellectual.
The term 'qawl' here is broader than speech; it includes mental statements.
2
Understand why 'composite' is specified
The author explains that without 'composite', one might mistakenly think qiyas is a part of propositions rather than composed of them. Reflect on the precision needed in defining logical terms.
3
Analyze the condition 'by itself' (lidhatihi)
The conclusion must follow from the premises alone, without extraneous premises. Note the exceptions: equality syllogism and contrapositive reasoning.
This ensures logical necessity is internal to the syllogism.
4
Distinguish audible from intellectual qiyas
Audible qiyas is verbal expression; intellectual qiyas is mental reasoning. The conclusion, even if expressed audibly, is ultimately an intellectual matter.
This distinction shows the universality of logical form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( القياس وعرف بأنه قول: أي مركب إما مسموع وهو جنس للقياس المسموع، وإما معقول وهو جنس للقياس المعقول، وإنما احتيج إلى قوله (مؤلف) لأنك إذا قلت: قول من قضايا تبادر منه أنه بعض منها، فصرح بأنه مؤلف من قضايا وأراد بها ما فوق الواحدة، متى سلمت تلك القضايا، سواء كانت مسلمة في نفس الأمر أو لا لزم عنه أي عن ذلك القول لذاته أي لا لمقدمة أجنبية غير لازمة لشيء من المقدمتين، كما في قياس المساواة أو غريبة لازمة لإحدى المقدمتين مغايرة لها في طرفيها، كما إذا بين اللزوم بعكس النقيض قول آخر أراد به المعقول لأن المسموع تحققه في جزئي مخصوص
Qiyas (syllogism) is defined as a statement (qawl), i.e., a compound (murakkab), either audible (masmu‘) which is a genus for audible qiyas, or intellectual (ma‘qul) which is a genus for intellectual qiyas. And it is needed for his word 'composite' (mu’allaf) because if you say 'a statement from propositions,' it would be understood that it is a part of them, so he clarified that it is composed of propositions. And he intended by 'propositions' more than one. 'When those propositions are accepted, whether they are accepted in reality or not, there follows from it' i.e., from that statement 'by itself' (lidhatihi), i.e., not from an extraneous premise not required for either of the two premises, as in the syllogism of equality, or a foreign premise required for one of the two premises but different in its two ends, as when he shows the entailment by contrapositive of the contradictory. 'Another statement' (qawl akhar), he intended by it the intellectual because the audible takes place in a specific particular.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas (syllogism) and explains its essential conditions. The definition states that qiyas is a statement (qawl) that is composite (mu’allaf), i.e., composed of propositions. The author explains that this statement can be either audible (masmu‘), meaning a verbal expression, or intellectual (ma‘qul), meaning a mental concept. The need for specifying 'composite' is justified: if one simply said 'a statement from propositions,' it might be misunderstood as a part of the propositions rather than a composition. Therefore, 'composite' is explicitly stated. The condition 'when those propositions are accepted' indicates that regardless of their truth in reality, the syllogism must be logically sound. The phrase 'by itself' (lidhatihi) means that the conclusion follows from the premises alone, not from an extraneous premise. Two exceptions are noted: the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat) and cases where entailment is shown by contrapositive of the contradictory (‘aks al-naqid). The conclusion is intellectual even if the premises are audible, because the universal form is an intellectual matter.

Key Terms
قياس
قول
مؤلف
قضايا
مسموع
معقول
لذاته
قياس المساواة
عكس النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Think that lidhatihi excludes all intermediate steps.
Correction: The condition 'lidhatihi' only excludes extraneous premises that are not required for either of the two premises. Intermediate steps that are logically entailed are allowed, as in equality syllogism and contrapositive.

Checkpoint
What is the primary definition of qiyas according to this passage?
A) A verbal statement.
B) A composite statement that necessarily follows from accepted propositions by itself.
C) A single proposition that implies another.
D) A metaphorical comparison.

Key Takeaway
The definition of qiyas requires it to be a composite statement that necessitates a conclusion from its premises by itself, without extraneous elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you explain your faith to others, do you ensure your conclusion follows necessarily from your premises, or do you rely on hidden assumptions that you have not examined? How can you apply the condition of 'by itself' to your own reasoning?
?
This lesson teaches that logical validity does not depend on the actual truth of premises. Are there arguments you have dismissed simply because you disagreed with the premises, without checking if the conclusion actually follows? How can you separate form from content in your evaluations?
?
The passage mentions that the conclusion of a syllogism is ultimately intellectual, not merely audible. Do you treat your beliefs as merely verbal affirmations, or have you internalized them as intellectual convictions that are 'grasped by the mind'? What is one belief you merely recite but have not truly understood as necessary from its evidence?

Three Types of Inference: Qiyas, Istiqra', and Tamthil
Classification of inference into three types based on direction of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of inference in Islamic logic: syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil), and how they differ by direction of reasoning.

Core Idea
Inference is classified by direction: universal to particular, particular to universal, or particular to particular.

In this passage, the author classifies inference into three types based on the direction of reasoning. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for evaluating arguments in kalam, as each type has different epistemic strength. We will see how qiyas moves from universal to particular, istiqra' from particular to universal, and tamthil from one particular to another via a shared cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal English translation. Let the author’s classification come through.
Notice the technical terms: qiyas, istiqra', tamthil.
2
Identify the three types and their definitions
The passage defines each type by the direction of inference. Find where qiyas goes from universal to particular, istiqra' from particular to universal, and tamthil from particular to particular via a common factor.
The definitions are given in sequence: ثم ... فهو القياس, فهو الاستقراء, فهو التمثيل.
3
Note the qualification on the difference
The author stresses that the types differ by consideration (حيثيات) not by essence (ذات). This means that with proper formulation, istiqra' and tamthil can be reduced to qiyas.
This is a key logical insight: the underlying structure can be syllogistic.
4
Reflect on application in kalam
Consider how these types of inference are used in theological arguments. Qiyas is the most certain, istiqra' gives probability unless complete, and tamthil is analogical.
This classification helps evaluate the strength of proofs.

ثم استدل منه على ثبوت ذلك الحال لأمر آخر، أو انتفائه عن ذلك الأمر لكونه جزئياً لذلك الكلي، ومندرجاً تحته فهو القياس، وإن كان المعلوم ثبوت حال الجزئي من حيث خصوصه ثم استدل منه على ثبوته للكلي بأن تتبع جميع جزئياته أو أكثرها، فعلم ثبوت ذلك الحال لها، ثم انتقل منه إلى ثبوته لذلك الأمر الكلي، فهو الاستقراء وإن كان المعلوم ثبوت حال الجزئي معين ثم استدل منه على ثبوته لجزئي آخر مندرج معه، تحت ثالث بأن علم علية الأمر المشترك لثبوت ذلك الحال في الجزئي المستدل عنه فوجد ذلك الأمر في الجزئي المستدل عليه فحكم بثبوت ذلك الحال له فهو التمثيل وبالجملة الفرق بين الأقسام باعتبار الحيثيات والاعتبارات لا بحسب الذات، حتى يصير الاستقراء والتمثيل أيضاً قياساً إذا جعل الأمر المشترك بين الجزئيات أوسط
Then he infers from it (the universal) the affirmation of that state for another thing, or its negation from that thing, because that thing is a particular of that universal and is subsumed under it — that is qiyas. And if the known is the affirmation of the state of the particular in terms of its specificity, then he infers from it its affirmation for the universal by surveying all its particulars or most of them, thus knowing the affirmation of that state for them, then he transfers from it to its affirmation for that universal thing — that is istiqra' (induction). And if the known is the affirmation of a state of a specific particular, then he infers from it its affirmation for another particular subsumed with it under a third, by knowing the causality of the common factor for the affirmation of that state in the particular argued from, then he finds that common factor in the particular argued for, so he judges the affirmation of that state for it — that is tamthil (analogy). In summary, the difference between the divisions is by consideration and perspectives, not by essence, such that istiqra' and tamthil also become qiyas if the common factor among the particulars is made the middle term.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage classifies inference into three types based on the direction of reasoning. The first type is qiyas (syllogism), where the universal is known and the particular is inferred by subsumption. The second is istiqra' (induction), where the state of the particular is known and then transferred to the universal by surveying all or most particulars. This yields certainty only if all particulars are surveyed; otherwise it yields probability. The third is tamthil (analogy), where the state of one particular is transferred to another particular based on a shared common factor (cause). The author emphasizes that these divisions are based on perspective and considerations, not on essence, meaning that induction and analogy can be considered syllogistic if the common factor is made the middle term.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Induction and analogy are not valid forms of inference or cannot be reduced to syllogistic logic.
Correction: The passage states that the difference is by consideration, not essence, so istiqra' and tamthil become qiyas if the common factor is made the middle term. Thus they are reducible to syllogism and valid.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does tamthil (analogy) differ from qiyas?
A) Tamthil goes from particular to particular via a common factor, while qiyas goes from universal to particular.
B) Tamthil is always certain, while qiyas is only probable.
C) Tamthil is not a valid form of inference.
D) Tamthil goes from particular to universal, while qiyas goes from universal to particular.

Key Takeaway
The three types of inference differ in direction but can be unified under syllogistic form by appropriate formulation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Which of these has the strongest hold on your certainty? Will you examine your own reasoning tonight to see if you've been using a weak analogy where a firm syllogism is needed?
?
The passage teaches that the difference between inference types is one of perspective, not essence. In your debates with others, do you assume your method is the only valid one? Can you name a specific instance where you dismissed someone's argument as 'just an analogy' without considering it could be recast as a valid syllogism? What would change if you treated their reasoning as a different perspective on the same logical core?
?
You regularly use tamthil (analogy) when explaining Islamic teachings—but do you verify that the common factor ('illa) truly applies? Think of the last analogy you used to explain a religious ruling. Did you check that the cause is identical? If not, are you spreading a false certainty? Resolve today to trace every analogy back to its common factor.

Qiyas as the Mainstay of Certainty
Defines qiyas as mainstay, explains its superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qiyas is considered the mainstay of reasoning in Islamic logic and how it differs from induction and analogy in yielding certainty.

Core Idea
Qiyas alone yields certainty; induction and analogy are subordinate.

In this passage, the author establishes qiyas (analogical reasoning) as the foundational method for achieving certainty. He distinguishes it from istiqra' (induction) and tamthil (analogy), showing that these methods only yield certainty if they are ultimately reducible to qiyas. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for grasping the role of logic in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how qiyas is defined as 'al-umda' (the mainstay) and the reason given.
The passage includes both the main text and a commentary.
2
Identify the three methods
The passage mentions qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil. Note how each relates to certainty.
Istiqra' is induction, tamthil is analogy.
3
Understand the conditions
Istiqra' yields certainty only if it is a 'divided qiyas'; tamthil yields certainty only if the cause is definitive. See how these conditions revert them to qiyas.
4
Reflect on the commentary
The commentary elaborates that istiqra' and tamthil revert to qiyas absolutely: what yields certainty becomes definitive qiyas, what yields probability becomes probable qiyas.
This reinforces qiyas as the mainstay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الرابع: القياس وهو العمدة

القياس وهو العمدة لإفادته اليقين فإن الاستقراء لا يفيد يقيناً إلا إذا كان قياساً مقسماً، وكذا التمثيل لا يفيده إلا إذا كانت العلة فيه قطعية وحينئذ يرجع إلى القياس

قوله : ( لإفادته اليقين) إذا كانت مقدماته يقينية بخلاف الاستقراء والتمثيل، فإنهما من حيث ذاتيهما لا يفيد أنه أصلاً .

قوله : ( وهو العمدة، لإفادته اليقين إلخ قد يقال : في وجه كون القياس عمدة إن الاستقراء والتمثيل يرجعان إليه مطلقاً أما ما يفيد اليقين منهما فراجع إلى القياس القطعي، وأما ما يفيد الظن فراجع إلى القياس الظني .
The Sixth Observatory - The Fourth Objective: Qiyas (analogical reasoning), which is the mainstay. Qiyas is the mainstay because it yields certainty; for istiqra' (induction) does not yield certainty unless it is a divided qiyas, and likewise tamthil (analogy) does not yield it unless the cause (illa) in it is definitive, and then it reverts to qiyas. Commentary: 'His saying: (because it yields certainty): That is when its premises are certain, unlike istiqra' and tamthil, for they in their essence do not yield certainty at all.' 'His saying: (And it is the mainstay, because it yields certainty etc.) It may be said: Regarding the reason why qiyas is the mainstay, istiqra' and tamthil both revert to it absolutely: what yields certainty from them reverts to definitive qiyas, and what yields probability reverts to probable qiyas.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas (analogical reasoning) as the mainstay (al-'umda) of reasoning because it alone produces certainty (yaqin). The author contrasts it with istiqra' (induction) and tamthil (analogy), which do not yield certainty by themselves. Istiqra' only yields certainty if it is a 'divided qiyas' (a complete enumeration? but here it is a type of qiyas). Similarly, tamthil only yields certainty if the cause (illa) is definitive (qat'iyya), in which case it reduces to a qiyas. The commentary elaborates: the certainty from qiyas depends on its premises being certain; istiqra' and tamthil in their essence do not give certainty. A further point: the reason qiyas is the mainstay is that both istiqra' and tamthil ultimately revert to it: what yields certainty from them is a definitive qiyas, and what yields probability is a probable qiyas. Thus the passage establishes the foundational role of qiyas in achieving certainty in reasoning.

Correction: The text clarifies that istiqra' yields certainty only when it is a divided qiyas, otherwise it does not.

Checkpoint

A) The major term must be universal
B) The cause (illa) must be definitive
C) The premises must be affirmative

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is the only method that yields certainty in itself; other methods depend on it for certainty.

Definition and Division of Premises
Defines premises and divides them into certain and probable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how premises are defined in kalam methodology as the propositions investigated in relation to the proof, and how they are divided into certain and probable categories.

Core Idea
Premises are the building blocks of proofs; they are either certain or probable.

Before we can assess arguments, we need to understand the raw material they are made of: premises. In this passage, the author defines premises as the propositions that are investigated in relation to the proof, and then immediately divides them into two types: certain and probable. This sets up the entire discussion of logical demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a general sense of the content. Notice the structure: first the heading, then the definition, then the division, then commentary.
The passage includes both the main text (matn) and the commentary (sharh). Focus on the main text first.
2
Identify the definition of premises
Find how premises are defined: 'the propositions in which occurs the investigation related to the proof, which is the path to assent.' Note that 'premises' implies they will become part of a syllogism.
The commentary explains that the term 'premises' is used because it is their nature to become part of a proof.
3
Note the division into two types
The passage states premises are of two types: categorical (qaṭ‘iyyah) used in certain proofs, and probabilistic (ẓanniyyah) used in indications. This classification is fundamental for evaluating arguments.
Understanding this division helps in assessing the strength of proofs.
4
Understand the commentary's clarifications
The commentary explains why investigation is limited to proof-related propositions (excluding definitions) and refutes the error that investigation occurs in both the whole and the part simultaneously.
This refines our understanding of how premises relate to proofs.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is it important to classify premises as certain or probable? How does this affect the validity of theological arguments?
This prepares you for later discussions on certainty and probability in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد السادس : في المقدمات ] أي القضايا التي يقع فيها النظر المتعلق بالدليل الذي هو الطريق إلى التصديق مطلقاً على قسمين قطعية تستعمل في الأدلة القطعية وظنية تستعمل في الإمارة. قوله : (أي القضايا إلخ) فإطلاق المقدمات عليها باعتبار أن من شأنها أن تصير جزء قياس أو حجة وفي توصيفها بقوله : التي يقع فيها إشارة إلى وجه إيرادها في المرصد المنعقد لمباحث النظر، وهو أنه مما يقع فيه النظر فيكون كالمادة له فمباحثها من تتمة مباحثه، وفي توصيف النظر بقوله : المتعلق إشارة إلى وجه تأخيره عن مباحث الدليل واحتراز عن النظر المتعلق بالمعرف فإن القضايا المذكورة لا تعلق لها به وقد عرفت من تعريف الطريق الموصل أن تعلق النظر بالدليل هو وقوعه في أحواله أو في نفسه فعلى الأول وقوع النظر في المقدمات هو وقوع النظر في الأحوال المثبتة للدليل أو المنفية عنه، وعلى الثاني الدليل نفس المقدمات فوقوع النظر فيها هو وقوعه في الدليل، وما قيل: إن النظر يقع في الكل والجزء معاً والقضايا جزء الطريق الذي هو الدليل فوهم، لأن ها هنا نظراً واحداً يقع في القضايا، ولا نظر يقع في الدليل ولو سلم فإنما يصح إذا جعل الدليل عبارة عن المقدمات المأخوذة مع الترتيب. قوله : ( مطلقاً) أي يقينياً كان أو ظنياً. قوله : ( على قسمين خبر مبتدأ محذوف أي هي على قسمين قدر هذا الكلام لتصحيح الفاء المذكورة في قوله فالقطعية.
[The Sixth Goal: On Premises] i.e., the propositions in which occurs the investigation (naẓar) related to the proof (dalīl), which is the path to assent (taṣdīq) absolutely, and they are of two types: categorical (qaṭ‘iyyah) used in categorical proofs, and probabilistic (ẓanniyyah) used in indications (imārah). His words: 'i.e., the propositions etc.': The application of the term 'premises' to them is because it is their nature to become part of a syllogism (qiyās) or an argument (ḥujjah). And in his description 'in which occurs' there is an indication of the reason for their presentation in the observatory (marṣad) established for the discussions of investigation (naẓar), namely that they are something in which investigation occurs, so they are like the matter (māddah) for it, thus their discussions are a completion of its discussions. And in his description of investigation as 'related' there is an indication of the reason for its delay after the discussions of the proof, and a precaution against investigation related to definitions (ma‘ārif), for the mentioned propositions have no relation to that. And you have already known from the definition of the connecting path that the relation of investigation to the proof is its occurrence in its states (aḥwāl) or in itself. Thus on the first, the occurrence of investigation in the premises is the occurrence of investigation in the states that affirm or negate the proof; on the second, the proof itself is the premises, so the occurrence of investigation in them is its occurrence in the proof. And what is said that investigation occurs in both the whole and the part, and the propositions are part of the path which is the proof, is an error, because there is only one investigation that occurs in the propositions, and there is no investigation that occurs in the proof. Even if granted, it would only be valid if the proof is taken to be the propositions taken with the arrangement. His words: 'absolutely': i.e., whether certain or probabilistic. His words: 'are of two types' is the predicate of a deleted subject, i.e., 'they are of two types', and this phrase is supplied to correct the fā’ mentioned in his saying 'al-qaṭ‘iyyah'.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth goal (al-maqṣad al-sādis) of the sixth observatory, which is on premises (al-muqaddimāt). The main text states that premises are the propositions in which investigation (naẓar) occurs, where the investigation is related to the proof (dalīl) that is the path to assent (taṣdīq) in an absolute sense, i.e., whether the assent is certain or probable. The premises are then divided into two categories: categorical (qaṭ‘iyyah) used in certain proofs, and probabilistic (ẓanniyyah) used in indications (imārah). The commentary then explains why the term 'premises' is applied to these propositions: because they are meant to become part of a syllogism or argument. It notes that describing them as 'in which occurs' points to the reason they are presented in the observatory dedicated to investigation — they are the matter (māddah) of investigation. The commentary also explains that restricting investigation to that 'related to the proof' justifies delaying this discussion until after the discussion of the proof itself, and excludes investigation related to definitions. It then clarifies how investigation relates to the proof: either by examining its states or by examining the proof itself which is the premises. A common misunderstanding is refuted: investigation does not occur to both the whole (the proof) and the part (the premises) simultaneously; rather, there is only one investigation focused on the premises. Finally, the commentary clarifies the grammar of the division statement.

Key Terms
المقدمات
القضايا
النظر
الدليل
التصديق

ظنية
الإمارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Investigation occurs simultaneously in both the whole proof and the premises as a part.
Correction: There is only one investigation that occurs in the premises themselves; the proof is either the states of the proof considered or the premises taken with arrangement.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are premises (al-muqaddimāt) defined as?
A) The certain conclusions of a syllogism
B) The propositions investigated in relation to the proof
C) The method of investigating divine attributes
D) The arrangement of terms in a syllogism

Key Takeaway
Premises are the propositions investigated in relation to the proof, and they are either certain or probable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your faith, do you rely on certain premises (from clear texts) or probabilistic premises (from weak opinions)? How does each affect your certainty and testimony?
?
This passage teaches that premises are the matter of investigation. How often do you examine the premises of your own beliefs? Are you willing to uproot a cherished belief if you find its premises are only probable, not certain?
?
The passage warns against confusing definitions with premises. Are there truths you have accepted as proven merely because they are well-defined, without verifying the premises that support them? Will you commit to distinguishing definitions from premises in your next serious study?

Definition of Certainty and Its First Condition
Definition of yaqin and first condition's exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of yaqin (certainty) as a belief that corresponds to reality and the types of knowledge it excludes.

Core Idea
Certainty is not just true belief; it must match reality.

The commentator begins by defining certainty (yaqin) according to the scholars of kalam. He clarifies that yaqin is not simply any belief, but one that corresponds to reality. This first part of the definition eliminates various types of false or unfounded beliefs, such as compound ignorance and blind following of error.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its explanation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that the definition of yaqin includes 'correspondence to reality' as a key component.
2
Identify the three exclusions
The commentator lists three things excluded by the condition of correspondence. Find and list them: compound ignorance, following the mistaken, and non-corresponding conjecture.
Each represents a type of belief that fails to match reality.
3
Reflect on why each is excluded
Think about why each of the three cannot be considered certain knowledge. Compound ignorance is false belief; following the mistaken relies on wrong authority; non-corresponding conjecture is a guess that doesn't match facts.
This condition ensures that certainty is based on truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقطعية ( أي اليقينية واليقين هو اعتقاد أن الشيء كذا مع مطابقته للواقع واعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن يكون إلا كذا. قوله : (مع مطابقته للواقع خرج به الجهل المركب وتقليد المخطئ والظن الغير المطابق. قوله : ( واعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن يكون إلا كذا فلا يحتمل النقيض أصلاً لا في الحال، فحرج الظن المطابق، ولا في المال فخرج تقليد المصيب، لأنه لعدم استناده إلى موجب يحتمل النقيض مالاً. قوله : ( والمراد إلخ) يعني ها هنا الضرورية بمعونة البيان .
Al-qat`iyya (i.e., al-yaqiniyya) and al-yaqin is a belief that a thing is such and such with its correspondence to reality and a belief that it cannot be except such. His saying: 'with its correspondence to reality' excludes compound ignorance and following the mistaken and non-corresponding conjecture. His saying: 'and a belief that it cannot be except such' so it does not admit the opposite at all, neither in the present, so corresponding conjecture is excluded, nor in the future, so following the correct one is excluded, because due to its lack of reliance on a cause, it admits the opposite in the future. His saying: 'and the intended etc.' means here the necessary with the help of exposition.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines certainty (yaqin) and explains its conditions as found in the main text. The commentator first states that certain premises (al-qat`iyya) are the same as certain knowledge (al-yaqiniyya). He then defines yaqin as a belief (i`tiqad) that something is such-and-such, provided it corresponds to reality, and additionally that the believer believes it cannot be otherwise. The commentator then explains each part of this definition. Regarding correspondence to reality, he says that it excludes three things: compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), where one believes something false; following the mistaken (taqlid al-mukhti’), where one follows an authority who is wrong; and non-corresponding conjecture (al-zann ghayr al-mutabiq), which is a guess that does not match reality. As for the condition that one believes it cannot be otherwise, the commentator says this excludes possibility of the opposite both in the present (al-hal) and in the future (al-ma’al). Thus, corresponding conjecture (al-zann al-mutabiq) is excluded because although it matches reality, it admits the possibility of the opposite. Also, following the correct authority (taqlid al-musib) is excluded because it lacks a proper basis and thus admits the opposite in the future. Finally, the commentator clarifies that the term 'certain' here refers to necessary certainties, not theoretical ones, and this is indicated by the phrase 'with the help of exposition' which points to the necessity of some mental effort.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think certainty is simply a strong belief, regardless of its truth.
Correction: Certainty requires correspondence to reality; a strong false belief is not certainty but compound ignorance.

Checkpoint

A) Corresponding conjecture
B) Compound ignorance
C) Following the correct authority
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
Yaqin begins with a belief that matches reality, thereby excluding all false or unfounded beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Second Condition: Impossibility of the Opposite
Second condition of yaqin and its exclusions, with final clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second condition of yaqin: the belief that the opposite cannot be true, and what this excludes.

Core Idea
Certainty also demands that the belief be held as necessarily true, without possibility of error.

Having established that certainty must correspond to reality, the commentator now explains the second essential condition: the believer must firmly hold that the matter cannot be otherwise. This condition eliminates two more types of belief: correct conjecture (which could be wrong in the future) and correct but ungrounded belief (taqlid of a correct authority). Finally, the commentator clarifies that the certain premises discussed here are those known by necessity after some exposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of the second condition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the second condition is 'a belief that it cannot be except such'.
2
Identify the two exclusions
The commentator says this condition excludes 'admittance of the opposite' in two respects: in the present and in the future. Corresponding conjecture is excluded in the present; following the correct authority is excluded in the future. Understand why each fails.
Corresponding conjecture matches reality but could be wrong now; taqlid of correct lacks proper basis and could fail later.
3
Understand the final clarification
The commentator notes that 'certain' here refers to necessary certainties (with exposition), not theoretical ones. This limits the scope of the definition to basic, undeniable knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقطعية ( أي اليقينية واليقين هو اعتقاد أن الشيء كذا مع مطابقته للواقع واعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن يكون إلا كذا. قوله : (مع مطابقته للواقع خرج به الجهل المركب وتقليد المخطئ والظن الغير المطابق. قوله : ( واعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن يكون إلا كذا فلا يحتمل النقيض أصلاً لا في الحال، فحرج الظن المطابق، ولا في المال فخرج تقليد المصيب، لأنه لعدم استناده إلى موجب يحتمل النقيض مالاً. قوله : ( والمراد إلخ) يعني ها هنا الضرورية بمعونة البيان .
Al-qat`iyya (i.e., al-yaqiniyya) and al-yaqin is a belief that a thing is such and such with its correspondence to reality and a belief that it cannot be except such. His saying: 'with its correspondence to reality' excludes compound ignorance and following the mistaken and non-corresponding conjecture. His saying: 'and a belief that it cannot be except such' so it does not admit the opposite at all, neither in the present, so corresponding conjecture is excluded, nor in the future, so following the correct one is excluded, because due to its lack of reliance on a cause, it admits the opposite in the future. His saying: 'and the intended etc.' means here the necessary with the help of exposition.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines certainty (yaqin) and explains its conditions as found in the main text. The commentator first states that certain premises (al-qat`iyya) are the same as certain knowledge (al-yaqiniyya). He then defines yaqin as a belief (i`tiqad) that something is such-and-such, provided it corresponds to reality, and additionally that the believer believes it cannot be otherwise. The commentator then explains each part of this definition. Regarding correspondence to reality, he says that it excludes three things: compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), where one believes something false; following the mistaken (taqlid al-mukhti’), where one follows an authority who is wrong; and non-corresponding conjecture (al-zann ghayr al-mutabiq), which is a guess that does not match reality. As for the condition that one believes it cannot be otherwise, the commentator says this excludes possibility of the opposite both in the present (al-hal) and in the future (al-ma’al). Thus, corresponding conjecture (al-zann al-mutabiq) is excluded because although it matches reality, it admits the possibility of the opposite. Also, following the correct authority (taqlid al-musib) is excluded because it lacks a proper basis and thus admits the opposite in the future. Finally, the commentator clarifies that the term 'certain' here refers to necessary certainties, not theoretical ones, and this is indicated by the phrase 'with the help of exposition' which points to the necessity of some mental effort.

القطعية
الظن المطابق
تقليد المصيب
الضرورية
البيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a true belief, even if reached by guessing or blind trust, is certain.
Correction: Certainty requires not only truth but also the firm conviction that the opposite is impossible; otherwise it is conjecture or taqlid.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the condition 'belief that it cannot be otherwise' exclude?
A) Compound ignorance
B) Non-corresponding conjecture
C) Corresponding conjecture and following the correct authority
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
True certainty requires both correspondence to reality and the belief that the opposite is impossible, eliminating conjecture and blind trust even if correct.

?
Think of a time you were absolutely certain about something. Did you actively rule out the opposite, or did you just assume you were right? What would it mean for your certainty if the opposite were actually possible?

Definition and Classification of Well-Known Premises
Sanusi defines mashhurat and enumerates their types with causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of well-known premises (al-mashhurat), their scope (universal, majority, group), and the six causes that lead to agreement on them, as well as their distinction from certain premises.

Core Idea
Well-known premises are accepted by agreement, not by certainty.

Sanusi introduces al-mashhurat as a category of non-certain premises that are widely accepted due to various reasons of agreement. This lesson unpacks his definition, shows how they differ from self-evident and apodictic premises, and lists the six causes that generate common assent. Understanding mashhurat is crucial for evaluating arguments that rely on popular consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and scope examples
Begin by reading the opening lines of the passage where Sanusi defines mashhurat and gives examples according to scope: all, majority, or a specific group. Notice the examples provided.
The examples help illustrate the range of mashhurat.
2
Identify what is excluded
The commentary clarifies that mashhurat exclude self-evident truths (awwaliyat) and apodictic theories (nazariyyat qat'iyya). Understand why these are not classified as mashhurat.
Mashhurat are non-certain; awwaliyat and nazariyyat qat'iyya are certain.
3
Learn the six causes with examples
Sanusi lists six causes for agreement: general benefit, compassion, zeal, religious disciplines, moral dispositions, and temperamental conditions. For each, study the example given and understand how that cause leads to widespread acceptance.
The examples are concrete: e.g., 'justice is good' for general benefit.
4
Note that the list is not exhaustive
The passage states that the enumeration is not exhaustive but illustrative. For instance, induction (istiqra') can also be a cause. Recognize that mashhurat can arise from other sources of agreement.
This prevents viewing the list as closed.

مشهورة عند الكل كقولنا : العدل حسن والظلم قبيح أو عند الأكثر كقولنا الإله واحد، أو عند طائفة كقولنا التسلسل مطلقاً محال وبالجملة فالمشهورات ما يحكم بها لتطابق الآراء عليها إما لمصلحة عامة أو رقة أو حمية أو تأديبات شرعية أو انفعالات خلقية أو مزاجية، سواء كانت صادقة أو كاذبة. قوله : (فالمشهورات) أي المعدودة من غير اليقينية، فخرج الأوليات المشهورة مثل الواحد نصف الاثنين والنظريات القطعية المشهورة مثل الله واحد. قوله : ( لتطابق الآراء كلها أو بعضها. قوله : (إما لمصلحة عامة) نحو العدل حسن والظلم قبيح أو رقة مثل مواساة الفقراء محمودة أو حمية مثل انصر أخاك ظالماً أو مظلوماً، أو تأديبات شرعية أي تطابق عليه الآراء لكونه مما أدب به الشارع مثل كشف العورة قبيح والطاعة محمودة أو انفعالات خلقية أي تابعة للخلق كقبح ذبح الحيوان عند حكماء الهند، أو مزاجية مثل دفع المؤذي واجب وليس هذا الترديد الحصر، بل بيان أسباب التطابق مثلاً فإن منها الاستقراء مثل التكرار المقصود من ممل على ما في المحاكمات.
Well-known (mashhurat) among all, like our saying: justice is good and injustice is bad; or among the majority, like our saying: God is one; or among a group, like our saying: absolute regress (tasalsul) is impossible. In summary, al-mashhurat are those judgments made due to the agreement of opinions upon them, whether for a general benefit, or compassion, or zeal, or religious disciplines, or moral dispositions, or temperamental conditions, whether true or false. His saying: (al-mashhurat) i.e., those counted among non-certain [premises], so the self-evident known ones (awwaliyat) like one is half of two and the apodictic theories (nazariyyat qat'iyya) like God is one are excluded. His saying: (due to agreement of opinions) all of them or some of them. His saying: (either for a general benefit) like justice is good and injustice is bad; or compassion like aiding the poor is praiseworthy; or zeal like support your brother whether oppressor or oppressed; or religious disciplines i.e., agreement of opinions upon it because it is something with which the Lawgiver disciplined, like uncovering the private parts is bad and obedience is praiseworthy; or moral dispositions i.e., dependent on character, like the ugliness of slaughtering animals according to the sages of India; or temperamental such as repelling harm is obligatory. And this enumeration is not exhaustive, but rather a statement of the causes of agreement, for example, it includes induction (istiqra') like the repetition intended from a dictation as in al-Muhakamat.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies well-known premises (al-mashhurat) in kalam. The author begins by giving examples of mashhurat according to their scope: universal (e.g., justice is good), majority (e.g., God is one), or specific group (e.g., absolute regress is impossible). He then formally defines mashhurat as judgments accepted due to agreement of opinions, regardless of truth value. The commentary clarifies that mashhurat are non-certain premises, excluding self-evident truths (awwaliyat) and apodictic theories (nazariyyat qat'iyya). The author then enumerates six causes of agreement: general benefit, compassion, zeal, religious disciplines, moral dispositions, and temperamental conditions, providing examples for each. The commentator stresses that this list is not exhaustive but illustrative, noting that causes can also include induction (istiqra') as in al-Muhakamat.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Mashhurat are non-certain premises; they are based on agreement of opinions and can be false. Certain premises like awwaliyat and nazariyyat qat'iyya are excluded from mashhurat.

Key Takeaway
Well-known premises are judgments accepted by agreement on the basis of causes like benefit or custom, yet they remain non-certain and can be false.

Three Types of Objects of Proof (Matalib)
The author divides the objects sought by proofs into three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of objects that proofs seek to establish and the respective roles of reason and revelation in each.

Core Idea
Objects of proof fall into three categories: known only by revelation, dependent on reason, or independent of both.

This passage from the matn introduces a fundamental classification in kalam: the types of objects (matalib) that proofs seek to establish. Understanding this division is crucial for knowing which theological claims require rational demonstration and which depend on revealed reports. The author lays out three categories with clear examples, highlighting the interdependence of reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Look for the three divisions: one that is possible according to the intellect, one that transmission depends on, and the remainder.
Notice the author uses 'أحدها', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
2
Understand the role of reason in each category
For each category, note whether reason can affirm or deny it, and whether revelation is needed. The first category cannot be known by reason alone; the second must be proven by reason; the third is independent of both.
The second category includes existence of God and prophethood – foundational for revelation.
3
Reflect on the examples and the significance
Consider the examples: a raven on a lighthouse (first), existence of the Maker (second), temporal origination (third). Why is this distinction important for avoiding circular reasoning?
The second category prevents the circularity of using revelation to prove its own foundations.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمطالب التي تطلب بالدلائل ثلاثة أقسام أحدها ما يمكن) عند العقل أي ما لا عقلاً إثباته ولا نفيه حتى لو خلي العقل وطبعه وترك مع ما عنده من اللوازم لم يحكم هناك بنفي ولا إثبات ( نحو جلوس غراب الآن على منارة الإسكندرية فهذا) المطلوب ( لا يمكن إثباته إلا بالنقل لأنه لما كان غائباً عن العقل والحس معاً استحال العلم بوجوده، إلا من قول الصادق هذا القبيل تفاصيل أحوال الجنة والنار والثواب ومن ،والعقاب، فإنها إنما تعلم بأخبار الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام ( الثاني ) من المطالب ما يتوقف عليه النقل مثل وجود الصانع وكونه عالماً قادراً مختاراً ) ونبوة محمد ) لله (فهذا المطلوب ( لا) يثبت إلا بالعقل إذ لو ثبت بالنقل لزم الدور ) لأن كل واحد منهما يتوقف حينئذ على الآخر ( الثالث ) من المطالب (ما عداهما نحو الحدوث ) فإن صحة النقل غير متوقفة على حدوث العالم (إذ يمكن إثبات الصانع دونه ) بأن قوله : ( فلا بأس إلخ) أشار به إلى أن الأولى عدم التسمية إذ لا فائدة في أفراد هذا القسم . قوله : أي ما لا يمتنع إلخ) لما كان المتبادر من قول المصنف ما يمكن عند العقل إثباته ونفيه أن يجوز العقل إثباته ونفيه، وذلك علم بالإمكان الذاتي وليس إمكاناً ذهنياً، فإنه عبارة عن عدم الحكم بالامتناع واستواء الثبوت والانتفاء عنده بحيث لا يتعين أحدهما، فسره الشارح بقوله : أي لا يمتنع من حيث العقل أي لا يحكم العقل بامتناع إثباته ولا بامتناع نفيه . قوله : (حتى لو خلي (العقل أي عن جميع العوارض الغريبة مقارناً طبعه أي حقيقته مع وترك مقارناً مع ما عنده من اللوازم لم يحكم هناك بنفي ولا إثبات، لأنه لما لم يحكم بامتناع الإثبات لم يحكم بالنفي ولما لم يحكم بامتناع النفي لم يحكم بالإثبات . قوله : (مثل وجود إلخ ) فإن صحة النقل تتوقف على صدق المخبر وهو يتوقف على ثبوت نبوته بإظهار المعجزة في يده وهو يتوقف على وجود الصانع، وكونه عالما حتى يخلق المعجزة على وفق دعواه، وكونه قادراً على خلق المعجزة ، وكونه مريداً يختار لمن يشاء من عباده بالنبوة على ما نطق به قوله تعالى ولكن الله يمن على من يشاء من عباده ﴾ [إبراهيم: ١١ ] . ( نزاع في كونه عقلياً فإن العصيان في اللغة ضد الطاعة، فلو أمر أحد غيره ولم يمتثل ذلك الغير لأمره يعد ذلك الغير الممتثل عاصياً، وإن لم يكن الأمر شارعاً، وقد يراد به استحقاق العقاب فهو حينئذ شرعي، فبالنظر إلى الأول عد صاحب المقاصد قولنا كل واجب فتاركه عاص، مقدمة عقلية وبالنظر إلى الثاني عد الشارح قوله : وكل تارك المأمور به عاص مقدمة شرعية لا عقلية من المعنى الأول كما توهم بل لأنه لو حمل عليه للغا الحمل، إذ يكون المؤدى تارك المأمور به تارك المأمور به اللهم إلا أن يفرق بين ترك المأمور به وترك نفس امتثال الأمر وإن تلازما. قوله : ( غير متوقفة على حدوث العالم فيه أن صحة النقل تتوقف على القدرة والاختيار
And the objects sought by proofs are three divisions. One of them is what is possible according to the intellect, i.e., what the intellect cannot affirm or deny, such as when the intellect is left alone with its nature and left with what it has of necessary consequences, it does not judge there either negation or affirmation. Like the sitting of a raven now on the lighthouse of Alexandria. So this sought cannot be established except by transmission, because since it is absent from both intellect and sense, knowledge of its existence is impossible except from the truthful one. This category includes the details of the states of Paradise and Hell, reward and punishment, for they are only known by the reports of the prophets, peace be upon them. The second among the objects is what transmission depends on, such as the existence of the Maker, His being knowing, powerful, and willing, and the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). So this sought is not established except by reason, because if it were established by transmission, there would be circularity, because each of them would then depend on the other. The third among the objects is what is other than them, such as temporal origination, for the validity of transmission does not depend on the temporal origination of the world, since it is possible to establish the Maker without it. His statement: 'then there is no harm' etc.: He indicated by it that it is better not to name it, since there is no benefit in distinguishing this division. His statement: 'i.e., what is not impossible' etc.: Since the immediate understanding from the author's statement 'what is possible according to the intellect to affirm and deny' is that the intellect allows affirming and denying it, and that is knowledge of essential possibility, not mental possibility. For it is an expression of not judging impossibility and the equal status of affirmation and negation such that neither one is determined. The commentator explained it by saying: i.e., it is not impossible from the perspective of the intellect, i.e., the intellect does not judge the impossibility of affirming it nor the impossibility of denying it. His statement: 'even if the intellect is left alone': i.e., free from all extraneous accidents, accompanying its nature i.e., its reality, and left accompanying what it has of necessary consequences, it does not judge there either negation or affirmation, because since it does not judge the impossibility of affirmation, it does not judge negation, and since it does not judge the impossibility of negation, it does not judge affirmation. His statement: 'like existence etc.': For the validity of transmission depends on the truthfulness of the reporter, and that depends on establishing his prophethood by the manifestation of a miracle at his hand, and that depends on the existence of the Maker, His being knowing so that He creates the miracle in accordance with his claim, His being powerful to create the miracle, and His being willing who chooses for whom He wills among His servants for prophethood, as indicated by His statement: 'But Allah selects for His mercy whom He wills' [14:11]. A dispute concerning whether it is rational: For disobedience in language is the opposite of obedience. If someone orders another and that other does not comply with his order, that non-complying person is considered disobedient, even if the commander is not a legislator. And it may be intended as deserving punishment, and then it is legal. So with regard to the first, the author of al-Maqasid considered our statement 'every obligatory, so its leaver is disobedient' to be a rational premise, and with regard to the second, the commentator considered his statement 'every leaver of the commanded thing is disobedient' to be a legal premise, not rational from the first meaning as thought, but rather because if it were carried upon it, the predication would be meaningless, since the consequent would be 'leaver of the commanded thing' 'leaver of the commanded thing'. Unless a distinction is made between leaving the commanded thing and leaving the compliance itself, even though they are concomitants. His statement: 'not dependent on the temporal origination of the world': In it, that the validity of transmission depends on power and choice.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage classifies the objects (matalib) that are sought through proofs into three kinds. The first is what is possible in the mind, i.e., the intellect can neither affirm nor deny it, such as the existence of a raven now on the lighthouse of Alexandria. Such matters can only be known through revelation, as they are absent from both reason and sense, like details of Paradise and Hell. The second is what revelation depends on, such as the existence of the Creator, His attributes, and prophethood. These must be proven by reason to avoid circularity (tawr). The third is what is other than these, such as the temporal origination of the world, which does not affect the validity of revelation. The commentator explains each: the first type is possible in the sense that the intellect does not deem impossible either affirmation or negation; the second type includes the existence of a Creator, His knowledge, power, will, and the prophethood of Muhammad; the third type is things like the world's origination, which can be proven without relying on revelation. The commentary also discusses a dispute about whether the premise 'every leaver of the commanded act is disobedient' is rational or revealed, noting that if disobedience means deserving punishment, it is revealed; if it means mere non-compliance, it is rational. The passage ends by noting that the validity of revelation depends on power and choice.

ما يتوقف عليه النقل
ما عداهما
العقل
النقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all religious truths must be proven solely by revelation.
Correction: The second category shows that foundational truths about God and prophethood require rational proof, otherwise revelation would be circular.

Checkpoint
