Key Takeaway
The classification of objects of proof into three categories clarifies the distinct roles of reason and revelation in theology, preventing circular reasoning and ensuring foundational truths are rationally established.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The first category includes details of Paradise and Hell. You will spend eternity in one of those places. Examine your heart: do you have any lingering doubts about the authenticity of the Quran or the Sunnah? Commit to studying one proof of prophethood today.

Are you one of them? When you hear arguments against God's existence, do you have a rational response, or do you default to 'I just believe'? This lesson challenges you to be intellectually responsible. What is one rational argument for God's existence that you can learn and internalize this month?

The First Type of Demand: What Only Transmission Can Establish
Defines the first type of demand and explains its reliance on transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of demands and the condition that defines the first type: a proposition that reason can neither affirm nor deny, requiring transmitted proof.

Core Idea
Some truths are inaccessible to reason alone and require transmitted reports.

Al-Iji divides theological demands into three categories based on the kind of proof they require. The first type consists of propositions that reason, left to itself, can neither affirm nor deny. These cannot be known except through transmission from a truthful source. This passage defines that category and illustrates it with a concrete example.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and classification
Read the first sentence carefully: the author states that demands are three divisions. Focus on the definition of the first: 'what reason can affirm and deny' — the commentator immediately clarifies that this means 'what reason does not deem impossible to affirm or deny.'
Notice the distinction between 'can' in a loose sense and the technical meaning of 'not impossible.'
2
Consider the example of the crow
The example given is 'the sitting of a crow now on the lighthouse of Alexandria.' Reflect on why this is something reason cannot decide: it is absent from both reason and sense, so we have no way to verify it directly.
This illustrates that the demand is contingent and particular, not accessible through rational deduction.
3
Identify the implication for religious knowledge
The passage then states that this category includes details of Paradise, Hell, reward, and punishment. These are known only through prophetic reports. Ask yourself: why does this classification matter for theology?
It grounds the necessity of relying on revelation for eschatological matters.
4
Check your understanding with the checkpoint
After reading, answer the checkpoint question to confirm your grasp of why transmission is necessary for this type of demand.

والمطالب التي تطلب بالدلائل ثلاثة أقسام أحدها ما يمكن عند العقل إثباته ونفيه أي ما لا يمتنع عقلاً إثباته ولا نفيه حتى لو خلي العقل وطبعه وترك مع ما عنده لم يحكم هناك بنفي ولا إثبات نحو جلوس غراب الآن على منارة الإسكندرية فهذا المطلوب لا يمكن إثباته إلا بالنقل لأنه لما كان غائباً عن العقل والحس معاً استحال العلم بوجوده، إلا من قول الصادق هذا القبيل تفاصيل أحوال الجنة والنار والثواب والعقاب، فإنها إنما تعلم بأخبار الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام. قوله : ( فلا بأس إلخ) أشار به إلى أن الأولى عدم التسمية إذ لا فائدة في أفراد هذا القسم . قوله : أي ما لا يمتنع إلخ) لما كان المتبادر من قول المصنف ما يمكن عند العقل إثباته ونفيه أن يجوز العقل إثباته ونفيه، وذلك علم بالإمكان الذاتي وليس إمكاناً ذهنياً، فإنه عبارة عن عدم الحكم بالامتناع واستواء الثبوت والانتفاء عنده بحيث لا يتعين أحدهما، فسره الشارح بقوله : أي لا يمتنع من حيث العقل أي لا يحكم العقل بامتناع إثباته ولا بامتناع نفيه . قوله : (حتى لو خلي العقل أي عن جميع العوارض الغريبة مقارناً طبعه أي حقيقته مع وترك مقارناً مع ما عنده من اللوازم لم يحكم هناك بنفي ولا إثبات، لأنه لما لم يحكم بامتناع الإثبات لم يحكم بالنفي ولما لم يحكم بامتناع النفي لم يحكم بالإثبات .
And the demands that are sought by proofs are three divisions: one of them is what reason can affirm and deny—meaning what reason does not deem impossible to affirm nor impossible to deny, such that even if reason were left alone with its nature and abandoned with what it possesses, it would not judge there with negation nor affirmation—like the sitting of a crow now on the lighthouse of Alexandria. So this demand cannot be established except by transmission, because since it is absent from both reason and sense, knowledge of its existence is impossible except from the statement of the truthful one. This category includes details of the states of Paradise and Hell and reward and punishment, for they are only known by the reports of the prophets, peace be upon them. His statement: “There is no harm, etc.” He pointed by it to that the preferable is not to name, since there is no benefit in singling out this division. His statement: “Meaning what is not impossible, etc.” Since what is understood from the author’s statement “what reason can affirm and deny” is that reason can affirm and deny it, and that is knowledge of essential possibility, not mental possibility, for it is an expression of not judging impossibility and the equivalence of affirmation and negation in its view such that neither is determined, so the commentator explained it by his statement: “Meaning nothing prohibits rationally affirming it nor denying it.” His statement: “Even if reason were left alone” i.e., free from all extraneous accidents, accompanied by its nature i.e., its essence, and left accompanied by what it has of necessary concomitants, it would not judge there with negation nor affirmation, because since it does not judge the impossibility of affirmation, it does not judge negation, and since it does not judge the impossibility of negation, it does not judge affirmation.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of demand (matlab) and explains its condition. The author classifies demands into three types based on which proof they require. The first type is what reason can neither affirm nor deny – it is not rationally impossible to affirm or negate. The commentator clarifies that this is not about essential possibility but about reason’s inability to decide: even if reason is considered in isolation from external factors, it makes no judgment. The example given is the current sitting of a crow on a specific lighthouse, which is inaccessible to both reason and sense. Therefore, such a demand can only be established by transmission from a truthful source, i.e., prophetic reports. This category includes eschatological details. The commentator then addresses the author's phrase 'there is no harm,' explaining that it is better not to name this division. He further elaborates on the meaning of 'what reason can affirm and deny,' distinguishing between the initial impression of essential possibility and the intended meaning of non-impossibility. Finally, he explains the condition 'even if reason were left alone' by detailing that reason, stripped of external influences, would still not judge either way.

Key Terms
المطلب (al-matlab: the demand/proposition sought)
الدليل النقلي (al-dalīl al-naqlī: transmitted proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the demand of the crow's sitting on the lighthouse only be established by transmission?
A) Because it is rationally impossible
B) Because it is absent from both reason and sense
C) Because it is a matter of faith
D) Because the crow is an unreliable witness

Key Takeaway
The first type of demand is what reason can neither affirm nor deny; such propositions require transmitted proof, not rational inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any beliefs you hold about the afterlife that you have derived from your own reasoning rather than from revealed texts? How does this passage challenge you to rely solely on transmission for such matters?
?
When you contemplate the joys of Paradise or the punishments of Hell, do you find yourself imagining details based on your own assumptions? What would it mean for you to submit your imagination to the specific reports of the prophets?
?
This passage teaches that some truths are simply beyond the reach of reason. In what areas of your faith do you find it hardest to accept that you cannot reason your way to certainty, and how can you practice trusting the truthful informant (the Prophet) instead?

Clarifying Essential Possibility vs. Mental Possibility
Clarifies the technical meaning of the phrase 'what reason can affirm and deny,' distinguishing essential from mental possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī) and mental possibility (al-imkān al-dhihnī) as it applies to the first type of demand.

Core Idea
Mental possibility is not objective possibility but reason's suspension of judgment.

The author's phrase 'what reason can affirm and deny' might initially be taken to mean that reason has the capacity to decide both ways—that is, essential possibility. But the commentator corrects this interpretation, explaining that the intended meaning is mental possibility: reason simply does not judge the matter to be impossible in either direction. This lesson unpacks that critical clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's clarification
Read the passage beginning with 'His statement: "There is no harm, etc."' through to the end. Notice the commentator first addresses a potential naming issue, then turns to the interpretation of 'what reason can affirm and deny.'
Pay attention to the technical terms: essential possibility and mental possibility.
2
Understand the distinction
The commentator explains that the initial understanding (essential possibility) is not what is meant. Instead, it is mental possibility: reason's lack of judgment of impossibility, and the equivalence of affirmation and negation from reason's perspective.
Essential possibility would mean the thing is possible in itself; mental possibility means reason has no basis to rule it out.
3
Examine the condition 'even if reason were left alone'
The commentator elaborates on the condition: even when reason is stripped of extraneous factors and considered with only its necessary concomitants, it still does not judge. This shows that the impossibility is not rational but epistemological.
This step cements that the first type of demand is not about objective possibility but about the limits of rational knowledge.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
After reading, answer the checkpoint to confirm you can distinguish essential from mental possibility in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

How would your conviction change if you saw your uncertainty as a limit of your own mind rather than a sign of the thing's nature?
?

What would it take to align your judgments with objective possibility?
?

Definition of Universal Matters and the Examples of Existence and Unity
Defines universal matters and illustrates with existence and unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of universal matters (al-umur al-'ammah) and how existence and unity serve as examples that apply across all categories of existents.

Core Idea
Universal matters are concepts that apply to all existents: Necessary, substance, and accident.

This lesson introduces the foundational notion of 'universal matters' (al-umur al-'ammah) in Islamic theology. These are concepts that are not confined to any one kind of existent but encompass all three divisions: the Necessary (God), substances, and accidents. The passage defines this category and illustrates it with existence and unity, while also addressing potential objections about their universality.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the opening definition of universal matters as 'that which is not specific to one division of existents'.
The author then clarifies that such concepts must include all three categories.
2
Examine the example of existence
Notice how existence is said to be a univocal term (mushtarak ma'nawi) that applies to all three categories without qualification. Consider the brief mention of al-Ash'ari's differing view.
The author dismisses al-Ash'ari's position as unique and not widely accepted.
3
Analyze the example of unity
Read the objection regarding unity: if a true one is required, only the Necessary is truly one, so how can unity apply to substances and accidents? Then follow the answer: a conceptual unity (wahdah i'tibariyyah) suffices for universality.
The author argues that inclusion does not necessitate actual instantiation in every individual.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these distinctions matter for theology. Universal matters are crucial because they provide a framework for discussing attributes that are true of all existents, such as existence and unity, which are foundational for arguments about God's existence and oneness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الثاني : في الأمور العامة أي ما لا يختص بقسم من أقسام الموجود التي هي الواجب والجوهر والعرض) فإما أن يشتمل الأقسام الثلاثة كالوجود والوحدة فإن كل موجود وإن كان كثيراً له وحدة ما باعتبار . قوله : ( كالوجود أي المبحوث عنه في هذا الموقف وهو الوجود المشترك، فإن من أحكامه أنه مشترك معنوي فلا يرد أنه يجب التقييدها هنا عند القائل باشتراكه احترازا عن مذهب الأشعري وإلا يحتاج إلى الاعتذار بأنه مما تفرد به الأشعري فلم يعتد به . قوله : ( فإن كل موجود إلخ ) يريد أن شمول الوحدة للثلاثة لا يتوقف على وجود الواحد الحقيقي من الموجودات الثلاثة، فلو فرض انتفاؤه وأن كل موجود كثير فله وحدة اعتبارية، وهذا يكفينا في شمولها للثلاثة وبما حررنا لك اندفع أن شمول الوحدة للثلاثة لا يتوقف على وجودها في كل موجود، إذ يكفي في ذلك تحققها في الواجب وفرد من الجوهر، وفرد من العرض وأن شمول الكثرة لكل موجود ينافي عدها مما يشمل الاثنين أما الأول فظاهر، وأما الثاني فلان شمولها الفرضي للثلاثة لا ينافي اختصاصها بالاثنين في نفس الأمر.
The Second Standpoint: On Universal Matters, i.e., that which is not specific to one division of existents, which are the Necessary (al-wajib), substance (al-jawhar), and accident (al-'arad). Thus, either it includes all three categories, like existence (al-wujud) and unity (al-wahdah), for every existent, even if multiple, has a certain unity in some respect. His statement: 'like existence' i.e., that which is investigated in this standpoint and it is common existence (al-wujud al-mushtarak), for among its rulings is that it is a univocal term (mushtarak ma'nawi), so it is not said that it must be qualified here for the one who holds it to be common, as a precaution against the school of al-Ash'ari, otherwise it would need an excuse that it is something uniquely held by al-Ash'ari so it is not considered. His statement: 'for every existent, even if multiple, etc.' he intends that the inclusion of unity covering all three does not depend on the existence of a true one among the three existents. For if we suppose its non-existence and that every existent is multiple, it has a conceptual unity, and this suffices for its inclusion of the three. And by what we have clarified for you, it is repelled that the inclusion of unity for all three does not depend on its existence in every existent, for it suffices in that its realization in the Necessary, one individual of substance, and one individual of accident. And that the inclusion of multiplicity for every existent contradicts its being counted among things that include two only. As for the first, it is apparent; as for the second, because its hypothetical inclusion of the three does not contradict its being confined to two in actuality.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines universal matters (al-umur al-'ammah) and provides the first two examples: existence (al-wujud) and unity (al-wahdah). The definition states that universal matters are those which are not restricted to one of the three divisions of existents: the Necessary (God), substance (jawhar), and accident ('arad). They must apply to all three categories. The author then explains that existence is a univocal term (mushtarak ma'nawi) that applies to all three without qualification, and this is the position of most theologians except al-Ash'ari, whose unique view is disregarded. For unity, an objection might be raised that if unity requires a true one, and the Necessary alone is truly one, then unity might not apply to substances and accidents. The answer is that we need not assume a real one; even if every existent were multiple, it still has a conceptual unity (wahdah i'tibariyyah). This suffices for unity to be considered universal. The argument then clarifies that inclusion does not require actual instantiation in every individual; it is enough that unity is realized in one individual from each category (Necessary, substance, accident). Furthermore, even if multiplicity (kathrah) applies to every existent, that does not prevent unity from being counted among things that include only two, because the hypothetical inclusion of the three does not contradict its actual confinement to two (as might be the case for some other concepts).

Key Terms
الأمور العامة
الوجود
الوحدة
الواجب
الجوهر
العرض
المشترك المعنوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: For a concept to be universal, it must actually exist in every individual of all three categories.
Correction: The author explains that it suffices if the concept is realized in at least one individual from each category, and conceptual applicability (e.g., conceptual unity) is enough.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does it mean for something to be a 'universal matter' (min al-umur al-'ammah)?
A) It is a matter that exists in every individual of all three categories of existents.
B) It is not specific to any one of the three divisions of existents (Necessary, substance, accident).
C) It is a matter that only applies to the Necessary.
D) It is a matter that is purely conceptual with no external reality.

Key Takeaway
Universal matters apply to all three categories of existents - God, substances, and accidents - and existence and unity are prime examples of such concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm God's existence and your own, do you genuinely accept that the word 'existence' means the same for both, or do you subtly treat God's existence as categorically different? How does your answer affect your sense of God's closeness or transcendence?
?
The author dismisses al-Ash'ari's view on existence as isolated and not worth addressing. Are there theological positions you have dismissed without truly understanding them? What would it require of you to engage with a view you find uncomfortable?
?
If unity is a conceptual matter that can apply to anything, what makes God's oneness unique? Have you ever stopped to consider whether your understanding of tawhid relies on a merely conceptual unity rather than a real, incomparable oneness? What would change if you took it as more than conceptual?

The Condition for Quiddity and Individuation as Universal Matters
Explains the condition for quiddity and individuation being universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that quiddity and individuation are considered universal matters only for those who affirm their distinction from existence in the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Quiddity and individuation are universal only if distinct from existence in the Necessary.

In this passage, the author addresses a key point in the discussion of universal matters: whether quiddity and individuation apply to all three categories of existents (Necessary, substance, accident). He explains that their universality hinges on one's metaphysical position regarding the Necessary Being's quiddity and existence. Let's examine the condition and the initial objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Look for the phrase 'إلا عند القائل بالتغاير' and understand its significance: quiddity and individuation are universal only for those who hold that they are distinct from existence in the Necessary.
This condition is crucial for the entire discussion.
2
Understand the objection
Note the objection: individuation is present in the Necessary regardless (whether identical to essence or not), so absolute individuation should be universal. The author presents this to highlight the need for the condition.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the author insists on the condition of taghayur: the absolute concepts of quiddity (neither existent nor nonexistent) and individuation (part of an existent) do not apply to the Necessary if they are identical to His existence.
This will be elaborated in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وكالماهية والتشخص عند القائل إلخ) أي الماهية والتشخص المبحوثان في الأمور العامة ليسا إلا ما يغاير الوجود حيث قالوا الماهية من حيث هي لا موجودة ولا معدومة وأن قوله : ( كالوجود) لا يخفى أن كون الوجود من الأمور العامة إنما هو على القول بالوجود المطلق وإنما لم يقيد كما قيد في الماهية والتشخص لأن نفيه مما تفرد به الأشعري فلم يقيد به . قوله : ( فإن كل موجود وإن كان كثيراً له وحدة ما فإن قلت: تعميم الكثرة لكل موجود ينا في عدها مما يشمل الاثنين فقط كما سيذكره الآن قلت التعميم فرضي وتلخيصه أن قوله وإن كان كثيراً معطوف على مقدر كما ذهب إليه البعض في مثله، والتقدير: إن لم يكن كثيراً له وحدة ما وبالجملة قولك أكرمك وإن أهنتني تعميم إكرامك لا يحقق إهانته فلا محذور، واعلم أن اعتبار الوحدة لكل موجود لا يحتاج إليه في بيان شمولها الأقسام الثلاثة، إذ يكفي تحققها في بعض من كل من الأقسام الثلاثة وإنما يحتاج إلى الاعتبار المذكور، إذا فسرت الأمور العامة بالأمور الشاملة لجميع الموجودات أو أكثرها وعد الوحدة من القسم الأول والفرق بين التفسير وتفسيرها بما لا يختص بقسم من أقسام الموجود بين كما يفهم من سياق كلامه في حواشي التجريد . هذا قوله: (وكالماهية والتشخص عند القائل إلخ) قيل عليه : إن تشخص الباري تعالى سواء كان عين ماهيته تعالى أو غيره فهو ثابت له تعالى، فيكون مطلق التشخص عاماً شاملاً له، وكذا الموقف الثاني : في الأمور العامة لوجوده وتشخص مغاير لماهيته أو يشمل الاثنين منها كالإمكان الخاص والحدوث والوجوب بالغير والكثرة والمعلولية فإنها كلها مشتركة بين الجـوهـر والعرض التشخص جزء من الشخص الموجود في الخارج وجزء الموجود موجود فلا يكونان من الأمور الشاملة للثلاثة إلا عند القائل بالتغاير فاندفع البحث المشهور من أن الماهية والتشخص يصدقان على الواجب سواء كان وجوده وتشخصه عين ماهيته أو غيرها، وأما ما قيل: الجواب أن الماهية تطلق على الحقيقة الكلية ولذا قيل : الماهية تدل على الكلية التزاما وهي من في الواجب، فليس بشيء لأن ذلك في الماهية بمعنى ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو وهو مصطلح المنطقيين دون بمعنى ما به الشيء هو هو المبحوث عنه في الأمور العامة كيف ولو كان كذلك لما صح قولهم تشخص الواجب ووجوده عين ماهيته. وهي منتفية قوله : ( والكثرة والمعلولية) فإن الواجب أعني ذاته تعالى لا كثرة فيه من حيث الأجزاء ولا من حيث الجزئيات والكثرة باعتبار الصفات ليست كثرة في الواجب، وكذا الحال في المعلولية فإن المعلول على تقدير زيادة الوجود وجوده تعالى لا ذاته المقتضية له فتدبر، فإنه زل فيه الأقدام . الكلام في الماهية سواء كانت عين وجوده أو لا وقد يقرر الاعتراض بعبارة أخرى، وهي أن القائل بأن ماهية الواجب تعالى عين وجوده وتشخصه هو الفلاسفة، وهم لا يقولون بعينية الماهية المطلقة والتشخص المطلق اللذين هما من الأمور العامة، بل بزيادتهما، ويمكن أن يؤيد الاعتراض بأن الشارح عد الماهية في حواشي التجريد من الشاملة للكل كالوجود والجواب أن الماهية تطلق غالباً على الحقيقة الكلية، ومن ثمة قيل لفظ الماهية يدل على الكلية التزاماً، والتشخص عندهم هو المنضم إلى الماهية ، ولذا صرحوا بأن التشخص غير الماهية، واستدلوا على ذلك وحكم المحققون بوجودية التشخص واستدلوا بجزئيته من المعين الموجود في الخارج فعلى تقدير كون تشخص الباري عز اسمه عين ماهيته وماهيته تعالى عين ،وجوده لم يوجد فيه ما يصدق عليه مفهوم الماهية المطلقة، والتشخص المطلق اللذين هما من الأمور العامة؛ وهذا ظاهر وما ذكره في حواشي التجريد مبني على إرادة الحقيقة من الماهية والحق أن الفرق بين الوجود والتشخص في كون الأول من الشاملة للثلاثة ولو قيل : بكون وجود الباري تعالى عينه كما يدل عليه عدم التقييد وعدم كون الثاني إلا على تقدير المغايرة خفي وسيرد عليك ما يفيدك بصيرة في المقام . قوله : ( وتشخص مغاير لماهيته لكنه غير داخل في هويته إذ لا قائل بالتركيب فلا ينافي هناك نفي الكثرة .
His statement: 'and like quiddity (al-mahiyyah) and individuation (al-tashakhkhus) according to the one who says, etc.' i.e., quiddity and individuation investigated in universal matters are only that which is different from existence, since they said: quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent. And his statement: 'like existence', it is not hidden that existence being among universal matters is only according to the doctrine of absolute existence, and it was not qualified as was qualified in quiddity and individuation because its denial is something uniquely held by al-Ash'ari, so it was not qualified by it. His statement: 'for every existent, even if multiple, has a certain unity' - if you say: the generalization of multiplicity to every existent contradicts its being counted among things that include only two, as he will mention now; I say: the generalization is hypothetical. And its summary is that his statement 'even if multiple' is conjoined to an implicit phrase, as some have held in similar cases, and the estimation is: 'if it is not multiple, it has a certain unity'. In short, your saying 'I honor you even if you insult me' generalizes my honoring you but does not actualize his insult, so there is no problem. And know that considering unity for every existent is not needed in explaining its inclusion of the three categories, since its realization in some from each of the three categories suffices. Rather, the mentioned consideration is needed if universal matters are interpreted as matters encompassing all existents or most of them, and unity is counted from the first group; and the difference between this interpretation and the interpretation of it as that which is not specific to one division of existents is clear, as understood from the context of his words in the glosses of al-Tajrid. This is his statement: 'and like quiddity and individuation according to the one who says, etc.' It was objected to him: Indeed, the individuation of the Most High, whether it is identical to His essence or other, is established for Him, so absolute individuation is general including Him. Likewise, the Second Standpoint: On Universal Matters, concerning His existence and individuation being different from His quiddity, or it includes the two (substance and accident) from them, like specific potentiality (al-imkan al-khas), temporal origination (al-huduth), existence through another (al-wujud bi-al-ghayr), multiplicity (al-kathrah), and being an effect (al-ma'luliyyah), for all of these are common between substance and accident. Individuation is part of the individual existing externally, and a part of an existent is existent, so they (quiddity and individuation) are not among the matters including all three except according to the one who holds difference (al-taghayur). Thus the well-known research is repelled: that quiddity and individuation are true of the Necessary whether His existence and individuation are identical to His quiddity or other. As for what was said in answer: that quiddity is applied to the universal reality, and therefore it was said that the word 'quiddity' indicates universality by implication, and it is negated from the Necessary, that is nothing, because that is for quiddity in the sense of that by which one answers the question 'what is it?' which is the terminology of the logicians, not in the sense of that by which a thing is what it is, which is investigated in universal matters. How so? For if it were so, their statement 'the individuation of the Necessary and His existence are identical to His quiddity' would not be correct. And they are negated. His statement: 'and multiplicity and being an effect' - for the Necessary, I mean His essence, has no multiplicity from the perspective of parts nor from the perspective of individuals, and multiplicity in terms of attributes is not multiplicity in the Necessary. Likewise is the case for being an effect, for the effect, on the assumption of the addition of existence, is His existence, not His essence that necessitates it, so reflect, for feet have slipped therein. The discussion about quiddity whether it is identical to His existence or not, and the objection can be formulated in another expression, namely that the one who says the quiddity of the Most High is identical to His existence and His individuation are the philosophers, and they do not say the identity of absolute quiddity and absolute individuation which are from universal matters, but rather their addition. And the objection can be supported by the fact that the commentator counted quiddity in the glosses of al-Tajrid among the all-inclusive like existence. And the answer is that quiddity is often applied to the universal reality, and hence it was said that the word 'quiddity' indicates universality by implication, and individuation according to them is that which is attached to quiddity, and therefore they explicitly stated that individuation is other than quiddity, and they argued for that, and the verifiers ruled the existence of individuation and argued by its being particular to a specific existent externally. Thus, on the assumption that the individuation of the Most High is identical to His quiddity and His quiddity is identical to His existence, there is nothing in Him that corresponds to the concept of absolute quiddity and absolute individuation which are from universal matters; this is apparent. And what he mentioned in the glosses of al-Tajrid is based on intending reality from quiddity. The truth is that the difference between existence and individuation in that the first is among the all-inclusive for the three, and if it is said that the existence of the Most High is identical to Him, as indicated by the lack of qualification, and that the second is only on the assumption of difference, it is subtle, and what will come to you will give you insight in this matter. His statement: 'and individuation is different from His quiddity but it is not included in His identity, since no one says composition, so it does not contradict the negation of multiplicity there.'
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage discusses whether quiddity (al-mahiyyah) and individuation (al-tashakhkhus) are universal matters. The author notes that they are universal only for those who hold that they are distinct from existence in the Necessary (i.e., that the Necessary has a quiddity other than existence). The objection is that individuation is true of the Necessary regardless of whether it is identical to His essence or not, so absolute individuation should be universal. The answer explains that quiddity and individuation as investigated in universal matters are the absolute concepts that include a lack of existence (for quiddity) or being part of an existent (for individuation). Since the Necessary has no such quiddity or individuation if they are identical to His existence, they are not universal unless one affirms a distinction (al-taghayur). The discussion then extends to the philosophers' view that the Necessary's quiddity is identical to His existence, and they do not affirm absolute quiddity as universal. The author defends the position by clarifying that quiddity in the logical sense (what is asked 'what is it?') is not the same as quiddity in the ontological sense (that by which a thing is what it is). The latter is what is meant in universal matters. The passage also touches on multiplicity and being an effect, noting that the Necessary has no multiplicity in parts or individuals, and being an effect applies to His existence, not His essence, on the assumption of addition of existence. The author concludes with a subtle point: existence is universal even if the Necessary's existence is identical to Him, but individuation is universal only if it is distinct from quiddity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that quiddity and individuation are universal matters regardless of one's view on their relation to existence in the Necessary.
Correction: The author shows that their universality depends on affirming a distinction (al-taghayur) between them and existence in the Necessary.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, under what condition are quiddity (al-mahiyyah) and individuation (al-tashakhkhus) considered universal matters (min al-umur al-'ammah)?
A) Only if one holds that they are identical to existence in the Necessary.
B) Only if one holds that they are distinct from existence in the Necessary.
C) They are always universal regardless of one's position.
D) They are never universal because they apply only to substances and accidents.

Key Takeaway
Quiddity and individuation are universal only for those who affirm their distinction from existence in the Necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent.' Does this shake your assumption that everything must either exist or not? How does this prepare you for the reality that Allah is beyond the categories of existence and non-existence as we understand them? Write one concrete change this makes in how you will think about Allah from now on.
?
This lesson shows that even the concepts we use to describe Allah are conditioned by our metaphysical assumptions. Are there areas in your life where you have unconsciously assumed that your human categories apply to Allah? How can you ensure your theology remains humble before His oneness?
?
The passage indicates that the universality of quiddity and individuation depends on a specific doctrine. Are you willing to hold your theological positions with humility, knowing that they are based on rational distinctions that might be questioned? How can you use this lesson to guard against intellectual arrogance in your pursuit of knowing Allah?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
8 min
The Response: Logical vs Ontological Quiddity
Resolves the objection by distinguishing logical and ontological quiddity and extends to multiplicity and being an effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between logical quiddity (that by which one answers 'what is it?') and ontological quiddity (that by which a thing is what it is), and how this distinction resolves the objection about quiddity and individuation.

Core Idea
Ontological quiddity, not logical quiddity, is what universal matters investigate.

The previous lesson presented the objection that quiddity and individuation should be universal regardless of the nature of the Necessary Being. Here, the author provides a detailed answer by distinguishing two senses of quiddity and explaining that universal matters concern the ontological sense, which is absent in the Necessary if His quiddity is identical to His existence. We will also see how this applies to other universal matters like multiplicity and being an effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of quiddity
Look for the distinction between quiddity as the answer to 'what is it?' (logical) and quiddity as 'that by which a thing is what it is' (ontological). The text says the latter is what is meant in universal matters.
This distinction is key to resolving the objection.
2
Understand the resolution
See how this distinction shows that absolute quiddity is not universal because it is negated from the Necessary if His quiddity is identical to His existence. The principle applies to individuation as well.
The Necessary does not have the ontological quiddity separate from existence.
3
Apply to multiplicity and being an effect
Notice how the same reasoning applies: multiplicity and being an effect are not correctly predicated of the Necessary's essence in a way that implies composition or dependency. The author warns that feet have slipped here.
Reflect on why these are not problematic for the Necessary.

Source Text

قوله: (وكالماهية والتشخص عند القائل إلخ) أي الماهية والتشخص المبحوثان في الأمور العامة ليسا إلا ما يغاير الوجود حيث قالوا الماهية من حيث هي لا موجودة ولا معدومة وأن قوله : ( كالوجود) لا يخفى أن كون الوجود من الأمور العامة إنما هو على القول بالوجود المطلق وإنما لم يقيد كما قيد في الماهية والتشخص لأن نفيه مما تفرد به الأشعري فلم يقيد به . قوله : ( فإن كل موجود وإن كان كثيراً له وحدة ما فإن قلت: تعميم الكثرة لكل موجود ينا في عدها مما يشمل الاثنين فقط كما سيذكره الآن قلت التعميم فرضي وتلخيصه أن قوله وإن كان كثيراً معطوف على مقدر كما ذهب إليه البعض في مثله، والتقدير: إن لم يكن كثيراً له وحدة ما وبالجملة قولك أكرمك وإن أهنتني تعميم إكرامك لا يحقق إهانته فلا محذور، واعلم أن اعتبار الوحدة لكل موجود لا يحتاج إليه في بيان شمولها الأقسام الثلاثة، إذ يكفي تحققها في بعض من كل من الأقسام الثلاثة وإنما يحتاج إلى الاعتبار المذكور، إذا فسرت الأمور العامة بالأمور الشاملة لجميع الموجودات أو أكثرها وعد الوحدة من القسم الأول والفرق بين التفسير وتفسيرها بما لا يختص بقسم من أقسام الموجود بين كما يفهم من سياق كلامه في حواشي التجريد . هذا قوله: (وكالماهية والتشخص عند القائل إلخ) قيل عليه : إن تشخص الباري تعالى سواء كان عين ماهيته تعالى أو غيره فهو ثابت له تعالى، فيكون مطلق التشخص عاماً شاملاً له، وكذا الموقف الثاني : في الأمور العامة لوجوده وتشخص مغاير لماهيته أو يشمل الاثنين منها كالإمكان الخاص والحدوث والوجوب بالغير والكثرة والمعلولية فإنها كلها مشتركة بين الجـوهـر والعرض التشخص جزء من الشخص الموجود في الخارج وجزء الموجود موجود فلا يكونان من الأمور الشاملة للثلاثة إلا عند القائل بالتغاير فاندفع البحث المشهور من أن الماهية والتشخص يصدقان على الواجب سواء كان وجوده وتشخصه عين ماهيته أو غيرها، وأما ما قيل: الجواب أن الماهية تطلق على الحقيقة الكلية ولذا قيل : الماهية تدل على الكلية التزاما وهي من في الواجب، فليس بشيء لأن ذلك في الماهية بمعنى ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو وهو مصطلح المنطقيين دون بمعنى ما به الشيء هو هو المبحوث عنه في الأمور العامة كيف ولو كان كذلك لما صح قولهم تشخص الواجب ووجوده عين ماهيته. وهي منتفية قوله : ( والكثرة والمعلولية) فإن الواجب أعني ذاته تعالى لا كثرة فيه من حيث الأجزاء ولا من حيث الجزئيات والكثرة باعتبار الصفات ليست كثرة في الواجب، وكذا الحال في المعلولية فإن المعلول على تقدير زيادة الوجود وجوده تعالى لا ذاته المقتضية له فتدبر، فإنه زل فيه الأقدام . الكلام في الماهية سواء كانت عين وجوده أو لا وقد يقرر الاعتراض بعبارة أخرى، وهي أن القائل بأن ماهية الواجب تعالى عين وجوده وتشخصه هو الفلاسفة، وهم لا يقولون بعينية الماهية المطلقة والتشخص المطلق اللذين هما من الأمور العامة، بل بزيادتهما، ويمكن أن يؤيد الاعتراض بأن الشارح عد الماهية في حواشي التجريد من الشاملة للكل كالوجود والجواب أن الماهية تطلق غالباً على الحقيقة الكلية، ومن ثمة قيل لفظ الماهية يدل على الكلية التزاماً، والتشخص عندهم هو المنضم إلى الماهية ، ولذا صرحوا بأن التشخص غير الماهية، واستدلوا على ذلك وحكم المحققون بوجودية التشخص واستدلوا بجزئيته من المعين الموجود في الخارج فعلى تقدير كون تشخص الباري عز اسمه عين ماهيته وماهيته تعالى عين ،وجوده لم يوجد فيه ما يصدق عليه مفهوم الماهية المطلقة، والتشخص المطلق اللذين هما من الأمور العامة؛ وهذا ظاهر وما ذكره في حواشي التجريد مبني على إرادة الحقيقة من الماهية والحق أن الفرق بين الوجود والتشخص في كون الأول من الشاملة للثلاثة ولو قيل : بكون وجود الباري تعالى عينه كما يدل عليه عدم التقييد وعدم كون الثاني إلا على تقدير المغايرة خفي وسيرد عليك ما يفيدك بصيرة في المقام . قوله : ( وتشخص مغاير لماهيته لكنه غير داخل في هويته إذ لا قائل بالتركيب فلا ينافي هناك نفي الكثرة .
His statement: 'and like quiddity (al-mahiyyah) and individuation (al-tashakhkhus) according to the one who says, etc.' i.e., quiddity and individuation investigated in universal matters are only that which is different from existence, since they said: quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent. And his statement: 'like existence', it is not hidden that existence being among universal matters is only according to the doctrine of absolute existence, and it was not qualified as was qualified in quiddity and individuation because its denial is something uniquely held by al-Ash'ari, so it was not qualified by it. In short, your saying 'I honor you even if you insult me' generalizes my honoring you but does not actualize his insult, so there is no problem. Thus the well-known research is repelled: that quiddity and individuation are true of the Necessary whether His existence and individuation are identical to His quiddity or other. As for what was said in answer: that quiddity is applied to the universal reality, and therefore it was said that the word 'quiddity' indicates universality by implication, and it is negated from the Necessary, that is nothing, because that is for quiddity in the sense of that by which one answers the question 'what is it?' which is the terminology of the logicians, not in the sense of that by which a thing is what it is, which is investigated in universal matters. How so? For if it were so, their statement 'the individuation of the Necessary and His existence are identical to His quiddity' would not be correct. And they are negated. His statement: 'and multiplicity and being an effect' - for the Necessary, I mean His essence, has no multiplicity from the perspective of parts nor from the perspective of individuals, and multiplicity in terms of attributes is not multiplicity in the Necessary. Likewise is the case for being an effect, for the effect, on the assumption of the addition of existence, is His existence, not His essence that necessitates it, so reflect, for feet have slipped therein. The discussion about quiddity whether it is identical to His existence or not, and the objection can be formulated in another expression, namely that the one who says the quiddity of the Most High is identical to His existence and His individuation are the philosophers, and they do not say the identity of absolute quiddity and absolute individuation which are from universal matters, but rather their addition. Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage discusses whether quiddity (al-mahiyyah) and individuation (al-tashakhkhus) are universal matters. The author notes that they are universal only for those who hold that they are distinct from existence in the Necessary (i.e., that the Necessary has a quiddity other than existence). The objection is that individuation is true of the Necessary regardless of whether it is identical to His essence or not, so absolute individuation should be universal. The answer explains that quiddity and individuation as investigated in universal matters are the absolute concepts that include a lack of existence (for quiddity) or being part of an existent (for individuation). Since the Necessary has no such quiddity or individuation if they are identical to His existence, they are not universal unless one affirms a distinction (al-taghayur). The discussion then extends to the philosophers' view that the Necessary's quiddity is identical to His existence, and they do not affirm absolute quiddity as universal. The author defends the position by clarifying that quiddity in the logical sense (what is asked 'what is it?') is not the same as quiddity in the ontological sense (that by which a thing is what it is). The latter is what is meant in universal matters. The passage also touches on multiplicity and being an effect, noting that the Necessary has no multiplicity in parts or individuals, and being an effect applies to His existence, not His essence, on the assumption of addition of existence. The author concludes with a subtle point: existence is universal even if the Necessary's existence is identical to Him, but individuation is universal only if it is distinct from quiddity.

Key Terms
الماهية
التشخص
الماهية المنطقية
الماهية الوجودية
الكثرة
المعلولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the difference between logical quiddity and ontological quiddity?
A) Logical quiddity is universal; ontological quiddity is not.
B) Logical quiddity is the answer to 'what is it?'; ontological quiddity is that by which a thing is what it is.
C) They are the same thing in different contexts.
D) Logical quiddity applies to the Necessary; ontological quiddity does not.

Key Takeaway
Universal matters deal with ontological quiddity, not the logical sense, and thus quiddity and individuation are not universal unless they are distinct from existence in the Necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is One,' do you ever reduce that oneness to a mere logical category? How can you ensure your affirmation of tawhid is grounded in the reality of His essence, beyond any universal concept?
?
The passage warns that 'feet have slipped' on this subtle issue. In your own study of aqeedah, where might you be confusing logical distinctions with ontological realities about Allah? What commitment will you make to guard against such errors?
?
If absolute quiddity and individuation do not apply to Allah, how does that reshape your understanding of your own limited knowledge of Him? Will you humble yourself before His transcendence and seek knowledge with the intention of drawing closer to Him, not just grasping concepts?

Why Non-Existence and Eternity Are Not Universal Matters
Commentator defends why non-existence and eternity are not universal matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-existence and eternity are not considered universal matters in kalam, and how the commentator defends this position against objections.

Core Idea
Universal matters must be actual properties of many existents; non-existence and eternity are not.

The commentator addresses a potential objection to the author's classification of universal matters. He argues that non-existence and eternity fail to be universal because they are not actual attributes found in multiple existents. This passage clarifies the logical criteria for universality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Context of the objection
The commentator begins by noting an objection: if non-existence and eternity are not universal, then discussing them is digressive. Understand why this matters.
The objection is based on the author's own definition of the subject.
2
Non-existence cannot be universal
The commentator argues that non-existence does not exist in any actual entity, so it cannot be a universal property. Notice how he addresses the Necessary Being, substances, and accidents.
3
Refuting the philosophical view
Some philosophers define substance and accident without actual existence, but the commentator refutes this, noting that in kalam they are originated and require existence.
4
Eternity is exclusive to God
Eternity, meaning lack of priority by non-existence, is only found in the Necessary Being, not in substances or accidents. The eternal attributes are a separate category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فعلى هذا إلخ) بيان للواقع واعتراض على المصنف بأنه يلزم أن يكون البحث عنها استطرادياً عنده، وهو لا يناسب جعله الموضوع المعلوم من حيث يتعلق به إثبات العقائد الدينية والأمور المذكورة داخلة فيه.
قوله : ( لا يكون العدم إلخ ) إذ لا يوجدان في شيء منهما فضلاً عن الشمول أما عن الشمول أما في الواجب فظاهر وأما في الجوهر والعرض فلأنهما عند المتكلمين عبارتان عن الحادث المتحيز بالذات، وعن الحادث القائم بالمتحيز بالذات على ما يجيء وما قيل من أن الجوهر عبارة عن ماهية إذا وجدت كانت لا في موضوع والعرض عن ماهية إذا وجدت كانت في موضوع، فلا يكون الوجود معتبراً فيهما بالفعل، فيشملهما العدم ففيه أنه من مصطلحات الفلاسفة على أنه نص الشيخ بأن الوجود بالفعل معتبر في العرض عندهم، والتعميم في الجوهر لإدخال صور الجوهر لا لأن الوجود ليس بمعتبر فيه كيف وقد قسموا الموجود الممكن إلى الجوهر والعرض كما سيجيء . قوله : ( والقدم) بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالعدم فإنه مختص بالواجب لا يوجد في الجوهر والعرض والصفات القديمة خارجة عنهما لما عرفت من تعريفهما .
His saying: ('according to this, etc.') is an explanation of reality and an objection to the author: that according to him, investigation of them would necessarily be digressive, which does not suit his making the known subject that by which religious beliefs are established, and the mentioned matters are included in it.
His saying: ('non-existence, etc.') because neither of them exists in any of them, let alone inclusion. As for inclusion: in the Necessary Being it is obvious; as for substance and accident, because in the theologians' terminology they are expressions for originated being that is extended in itself, and originated being subsisting in what is extended in itself, as will come. And what is said: that substance is a quiddity that when it exists, it is not in a subject, and accident is a quiddity that when it exists, it is in a subject — so existence would not be considered in them actually, so non-existence would encompass them. But it is replied: that is from the terminology of the philosophers, and the Shaykh states that actual existence is considered in accident according to them, and the generalization in substance is to include forms of substance, not because existence is not considered in it. Indeed, they divided the possible existent into substance and accident, as will come.
His saying: ('and eternity') meaning lack of priority by non-existence — it is peculiar to the Necessary Being, not found in substance and accident; and the eternal attributes are outside them due to what you know of their definition.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the author's statement that non-existence, impossibility, essential necessity, and eternity are not universal matters. The commentator first notes an objection: that if these are not universal, then investigating them in this context would be digressive, which undermines the author's own definition of the subject. Then he explains why non-existence cannot be universal: it does not actually exist in any existent. He considers an objection that in the philosophers' view, substance and accident are defined without actual existence, so non-existence could include them. But he refutes this by noting that it is the theologians' view that substance and accident are originated and require actual existence. Then he addresses eternity: if it means uncaused eternity, it belongs only to God, not to substances or accidents. The eternal attributes are separate from these categories.

القدم
الأمور العامة
الجوهر
العرض
الواجب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that non-existence is a universal predicate because it applies to many things.
Correction: The commentator shows that non-existence is not a real property of existents; universals must be actual attributes found in multiple individuals.

Checkpoint
Why is non-existence not considered a universal matter according to the commentator?
A) Because non-existence is not a real attribute of any existent.
B) Because non-existence applies to substances and accidents.
C) Because non-existence is a property of the Necessary Being.
D) Because non-existence is included in the definition of substance.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence and eternity are not universal matters because they are not actual properties of multiple existents, and the commentator refutes objections based on philosophical definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about 'non-existence', do you treat it as something that can be predicated of many things, like a universal property? ?

?
The commentator refutes a philosophical definition of substance and accident that ignores actual existence. Are there areas in your own thinking where you treat abstract concepts as if they have reality apart from existence? How can you ground your thoughts in the reality of Allah's creation and the theological framework of Ahl al-Sunna?

The Definition of Universal Matters and Its Two Modes
Sanusi defines universal matters and explains the two modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how universal matters are defined either absolutely (like general possibility) or by correlation (like existence and non-existence), and why general possibility is investigated through its concomitants.

Core Idea
Universal matters are the most general concepts of kalam, covering all rational judgments.

The commentator begins by clarifying the key terms in the definition of universal matters. He explains that these are concepts that encompass all others either singly or in pairs, and shows how the study of general possibility is actually carried out through its particular instantiations. This lesson lays the foundation for understanding the scope of kalam's subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Carefully read the first two sentences where the commentator defines universal matters and gives the two modes. Notice the mention of the three concepts: necessary, impossible, and possible.
The definition is highly compressed; take time to parse it.
2
Understand the two modes
The commentator distinguishes between absolute universality (a single concept covering all) and correlative universality (a pair covering all). Identify which examples belong to each mode.
Absolute: general possibility. Correlative: existence and non-existence.
3
See how general possibility is investigated
The commentator explains that general possibility is studied by investigating its concomitants (specific possibility, necessity, impossibility). This refutes the idea that general possibility is not part of kalam.
This shows that general possibility is a proper subject of investigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( المفهومات ( أي الواجب والممتنع والممكن.
قوله : ( على سبيل الإطلاق كالإمكان العام أو على سبيل التقابل بأن يكون هو مع ما يقابله متناولاً لها جميعاً، ويتعلق بكل من هذين المتقابلين غرض علمي كالوجود والعدم ) .
قوله : ( كالإمكان العام والبحث عنه عبارة عن حمل عوارضه اللاحقة له باعتبار تحققه في أفراده من الإمكان الخاص والوجوب والامتناع فيكون البحث عنها بحثاً عنه، فاندفع أنه لا بحث في الأمور العامة عن الإمكان العام .
قوله : ( ويتعلق بكل من إلخ قيد بذلك ليخرج كل مفهوم مع ما يقابله لشمولهما جميع المفهومات سواء لا يتعلق بشيء منهما غرض علمي كالإنسان واللا إنسان أو يتعلق بأحدهما دون الأخر، كالوجوب واللا وجوب ومعنى تعلق الغرض العلمي به أن يتعلق به إثبات العقائد الدينية تعلقاً قريباً أو بعيداً ، وإنما صرح باعتبار هذا القيد في هذا القسم مع أن اعتباره في جميع المباحث معلوم مما سبق في تعريف موضوع الكلام ، ولذا لم يصرح به في التعريف المذكور في المتن ولا في القسم الأول من هذا التعريف دفعاً لتوهم أن تعلق الغرض العلمي بأحد المتقابلين كاف في عدهما من الأمور العامة .
قوله : ( كالوجود والعدم لا خفاء في أنه إما أن يتعلق بالعدم غرض علمي، فلا يصح تعريف المتن حيث يستلزم كون البحث عنه استطرادياً، أو لا يتعلق فلا يكون هذا التعريف صحيحاً.
His saying: ('the concepts') i.e., the necessary, the impossible, and the possible.
His saying: ('either absolutely, like general possibility, or by correlation, such that it together with its opposite encompasses all of them, and each of these two correlatives has a scientific purpose, like existence and non-existence').
His saying: ('like general possibility') and investigation of it is to carry its concomitant properties that attach to it in consideration of its realization in its individuals, such as specific possibility, necessity, and impossibility; so the investigation of them is an investigation of it, thus it is refuted that there is no investigation in universal matters of general possibility.
His saying: ('and each of them has, etc.') is a restriction to exclude every concept together with its opposite when both together encompass all concepts but neither has a scientific purpose, like human and non-human, or only one of them has a purpose, like necessity and non-necessity. The meaning of having a scientific purpose is that it serves to establish religious beliefs, either directly or indirectly. He explicitly considered this restriction in this section, although its consideration in all investigations is known from the earlier definition of the subject of kalam; thus he did not mention it in the matn's definition nor in the first part of this definition, in order to prevent the misunderstanding that a scientific purpose in one of the two correlatives suffices for counting both as universal matters.
His saying: ('like existence and non-existence') there is no doubt that either non-existence has a scientific purpose — then the matn's definition would be invalid because it would entail that investigation of it is digressive — or it does not, then this definition is incorrect.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage defines universal matters (al-umur al-ammah) and explains its two modes. The commentator begins by clarifying that 'concepts' (al-mafhumāt) in the definition refer to the necessary, impossible, and possible. He then states that universal matters are of two types: (1) absolute universality, like general possibility (al-imkān al-ʿāmm), which is a single concept covering all things; (2) correlative universality, where a concept together with its opposite covers all things, and each has a scientific purpose, such as existence and non-existence. The commentator explains why general possibility is treated: investigating its concomitants (specific possibility, necessity, impossibility) is really investigating it, so it is valid. He then stresses the condition that each correlative must have a scientific purpose (gharaḍ ʿilmī), meaning it aids in establishing religious beliefs. This restriction excludes pairs like human and non-human, which have no theological use. Finally, he addresses an objection about non-existence: if non-existence has no scientific purpose, the definition fails; but if it does (since stating nonexistence helps define existence), then it is permissible. The commentator notes that the author did not explicitly mention this condition in the matn to avoid confusion, but it is implied.

Key Terms
الأمور العامة
الإمكان العام
التقابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: General possibility is not a proper subject of investigation in kalam because it is too abstract.
Correction: The commentator shows that general possibility is investigated through its concomitants, so it is indeed a legitimate topic.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is meant by 'concepts' in the definition of universal matters?
A) Existence and non-existence
B) The necessary, impossible, and possible
C) General possibility only
D) All opposites

Key Takeaway
Universal matters are the most general concepts, either single (like general possibility) or in opposite pairs (like existence and non-existence), and each must have a scientific purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you see it as an instance of 'general possibility'—something that could be or not be, entirely dependent on the Necessary Being? How does this realization affect the way you approach your daily decisions and your need for God? This week, before each major choice, pause and remind yourself: 'I am possible only because God makes me possible.'
?
The passage teaches that every correlative pair must have a 'scientific purpose' (gharaḍ ʿilmī). Reflect: What is the purpose of your pursuit of knowledge? Is it merely to accumulate facts, or do you seek to understand existence and non-existence for the sake of knowing the Creator? Commit to studying Islamic theology with the intention of drawing nearer to Allah, not just academic curiosity.
?
General possibility is investigated through its particulars—specific possibility, necessity, and impossibility. How often do you examine the specific possibilities in your life (your talents, circumstances) and recognize that they are expressions of the general possibility that only God controls? Are you using what has been made possible for you to serve Him? Take one concrete step today to use a gift or opportunity specifically for Allah's sake.

The Condition of Scientific Purpose in Correlative Concepts
Sanusi elaborates on the condition of scientific purpose and resolves an objection about non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the requirement that each member of a correlative pair must have a scientific purpose, and why the example of existence and non-existence is valid despite non-existence seeming to lack purpose.

Core Idea
Not every opposite pair qualifies as a universal matter; each must directly or indirectly serve theological inquiry.

The commentator elaborates on the restriction that each of the two correlative concepts must have a scientific purpose (gharaḍ ʿilmī), meaning they help establish religious beliefs. He gives examples of pairs that do not qualify, such as human and non-human, and addresses an important objection: does non-existence have a scientific purpose? This lesson clarifies the condition and resolves the apparent difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the restriction on correlative pairs
Carefully read the sentence that begins with 'قوله : ( ويتعلق بكل من إلخ)'. The commentator explains that this restriction excludes pairs where neither has a scientific purpose, or only one does.
Pay attention to the examples: human/non-human and necessity/non-necessity.
2
Understand the meaning of scientific purpose
The commentator defines scientific purpose as serving to establish religious beliefs, either directly or indirectly. This is a key concept.
This definition ties universal matters directly to the goal of kalam.
3
Analyze the objection about non-existence
The commentator raises an objection: if non-existence has no scientific purpose, the definition fails. He implies that it does have a purpose because stating non-existence helps define existence, which is necessary for theology.
This shows the indirect role of non-existence in theological arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islamic theology, do you take time to ask yourself: 'What purpose does this concept serve for my faith?' Are you studying topics out of habit or because they genuinely strengthen your belief in Allah? Commit to evaluating every new concept with this question.
?
This lesson teaches that even 'negative' concepts like non-existence have a purpose because they help define their opposites. Think of a belief you hold strongly—what is its opposite that you might be avoiding? How could embracing that opposite—not as true, but as a contrast—deepen your understanding of your own belief?
?
Sanusi warns against assuming that if one correlative has purpose, the pair counts. In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes rely on only one perspective (e.g., God's mercy) while ignoring the other (e.g., His justice)? How can considering both sides together lead to a more complete and humble faith?

The Second Possibility: The State as an Intermediate
Explains the second possibility affirming the intermediate state (al-ḥāl) and defines it as an attribute of an existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of al-ḥāl as an intermediate state between existence and non-existence, its definition, and the scholars who affirmed it.

Core Idea
The state (al-ḥāl) is a real attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent but depends on an existent.

In the previous lesson, we saw the first possibility that only the existent is real. Now we turn to the second possibility, which affirms an intermediate category called 'the state' (al-ḥāl). This view was championed by al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī and initially by Imām al-Ḥaramayn, as well as some Muʿtazila. Let's examine how they defined the state and how it relates to the existent and non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponents
Read the passage and note the scholars who held the second possibility: al-Bāqillānī, Imām al-Ḥaramayn (initially), and some Muʿtazila. Recognize that Imām al-Ḥaramayn later retracted.
The passage says 'Imām al-Ḥaramayn (from us) held it initially, then he retracted.'
2
Understand the tripartite division
According to this view, the known is divided into three: the non-existent (no actuality), the existent (actuality by essence), and the state (actuality dependent on another). Grasp the distinction between self-subsistent and dependent actuality.
The non-existent has no actuality at all; the existent has actuality by itself; the state has actuality only through another.
3
Define al-ḥāl
The passage defines al-ḥāl as 'an attribute of an existent, neither existent nor non-existent.' Understand why essences cannot be states: they are either existent or non-existent, not dependent.
The definition explicitly distinguishes al-ḥāl from the existent and non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الاحتمال الثاني المعدوم ليس بثابت والواسطة (أمر حق) أي ثابت (وقال به القاضي الباقلاني قولاً مستمراً (وإمام الحرمين (منا) أي من الأشاعرة (أولاً) فإنه رجع عن ذلك آخراً وقال به بعض المعتزلة أيضاً (فالمعلوم) على رأيهم (إما لا تحقق أصلاً (وهو المعدوم) أو له تحقق إما باعتبار ذاته أي لا بتبعية الغير (وهو الموجود) أو باعتبار غيره (أي له تحقق تبعاً له) وهو الحال وعرفوه بأنه صفة لموجود لا موجودة ولا معدومة فقولنا صفة لأن الذوات وهي الأمور القائمة بأنفسها (إما موجودة أو معدومة لا غير) إذ لا يتصور تحققها تبعاً لغيرها فلا تكون حالاً
(The second possibility: The non-existent is not fixed, and the intermediate is a real thing, i.e. fixed. Al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī held it consistently, and Imām al-Ḥaramayn (from us, i.e. from the Ashʿarīs) held it initially, then he retracted from that later. Some Muʿtazila also held it. So the known, according to their view, is either that which has no actuality at all (the non-existent), or it has actuality either by virtue of its essence (i.e. not by dependence on another) (the existent), or by virtue of another (i.e. it has actuality dependently) which is the state (al-ḥāl). And they defined it as an attribute of an existent, neither existent nor non-existent. For we say 'attribute' because essences, which are things subsistent by themselves, are either existent or non-existent only, since they cannot be conceived as having actuality dependently on another, so they are not states.)
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the second possibility, which holds that although the non-existent is not fixed, there is an intermediate state (al-ḥāl) that is a real thing. This was held by al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī, Imām al-Ḥaramayn (initially), and some Muʿtazila. According to this view, the known is divided into three: the non-existent (no actuality), the existent (actuality by essence), and the state (actuality dependent on another). The state is then defined as an attribute of an existent that is neither existent nor non-existent. The reason for calling it an attribute is that essences (dhawāt), which are self-subsistent, can only be existent or non-existent, not dependent, so they cannot be states.

Key Terms
الحال
المعدوم
الموجود
الصفة
التحقق الذاتي
التحقق التبعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the state (al-ḥāl) is a third kind of existence, like an entity that exists independently.
Correction: The state is not an existent; it is an attribute that has dependent actuality, neither existent nor non-existent in the strict sense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of al-ḥāl (the state)?
A) A self-subsistent entity
B) An attribute of an existent that is neither existent nor non-existent
C) A non-existent that has no actuality
D) An existent that depends on another

Key Takeaway
The state (al-ḥāl) is an attribute that has dependent actuality, neither existent nor non-existent, and is affirmed by some theologians as an intermediate category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something in your life that feels real yet intangible—like a reputation, a promise, or a hope. Is it existent or non-existent? How does the concept of al-ḥāl help you articulate its reality? Write down one such thing and describe its dependence on something else.
?
The state has no independent existence; it relies entirely on its subject. In what ways are your own attributes (e.g., knowledge, patience) dependent on Allah? How does this lesson strip you of any claim to self-sufficiency? Take a moment to acknowledge your complete dependence on Him.
?
If the state is neither existent nor non-existent, what does that say about your assumptions of what is 'real'? Are you willing to accept that reality is richer than a simple binary? Confess one area where you have reduced God's attributes or His creation to a mere 'yes or no' and commit to exploring the depth of reality more humbly.

Existence: Original External vs Shadowy Mental
Definition and differentiation of external and mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam definition of external existence as original (aṣīl) and mental existence as shadowy (ẓillī), and how they are distinguished by their effects and manner of location.

Core Idea
External existence is original and produces effects; mental existence is shadowy and derivative.

After a series of objections about the location of particulars, the author presents a straightforward definition: existence is either original, producing its sought-after effects, or shadowy, lacking such effects. This lesson unpacks that definition and clarifies the key distinction between external and mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Note the author's preferred definition after declaring earlier approaches strained.
The definition is marked by 'al-aẓhar an yuqāl' (the most apparent is to say).
2
Identify the two types of existence
Find where the author defines external existence as original (aṣīl) and mental existence as shadowy (ẓillī). Notice the brief criteria given.
External existence produces effects; mental existence does not.
3
Understand the criterion of effects
The passage explains that 'effects' mean sought-after effects like fire burning. Mental existence only has secondary intelligibles.
This answers the potential objection that mental existence also has effects.
4
Review the summary of the difference
The author summarizes: external existence is located by reality and individuation together; mental existence is located by reality alone.
This clarifies how to distinguish them in practice.
